RATING: 8
Lots of fun for a D&D-lite experience.

It's not the deepest, nor the most tactical game on the market, but for those with a limited amount of time for gaming who are looking to scratch that D&D itch, look no further than Castle Ravenloft.

Those looking for something a lot more epic, with sprawling campaigns and persistent RPG choices and experiences need not apply.

The hardcore will be disappointed.

But for the casual gamers our there, this should be a staple in your collection of dungeon crawlers.

RATING: N/A
First impression: No chance to play yet but it's hard to believe how much stuff is packed into the box. The box must be 6-7 inches in height and it needs that depth with all the cards, counters, puzzle boards, and plastic minis included. It's like a whole Christmas in a box!

Second impression: Well I played the first solo adventure and it was very intense (and fun!). Got at least one rule wrong which probably cost me the game. There are some vagaries in the rules that need to be addressed but not so many that a small FAQ couldn't resolve.

Final impression: too light for me. There's not enough hard choices and the system tends to play itself. Production values are outstanding but the game system is very much a novice level. (Sold it)

RATING: 6
*(Solo) 
My first dungeon crawler. Good as a primer for D&D4E.

I bought this when I was playing 4th Edition D&D heavily. The Adventure system would make a great primer for the RPG, which is pretty moot now that it is moving to 5th edition. 

The game itself is pretty rough. It punishes you for exploring. It punishes you for not exploring. Wave after wave of minions will beat you down on your way to fight a boss who will most likely kill you. Advancement options are a bit underwhelming when compared to others in the genre. 

I think Runebound is better for an RPG fix, and dungeon run better for dude in a dungeon fix.

RATING: 5
One of those love or hate games. For me, its more of a hate type.

The rules are very condensed and simplified till the rules make no sense. You will find, the identification of tokens are not clear, the monsters gives no 'power' difference in terms of which monster is better than others. (Important, as there are cards that 'forces' you to choose the stronger monster....how do you define that?)

The monster cards are not well defined as 'miss damage' are not clear. Treasure cards should state when a card should/could be played. Common sense does not equate correct usage of card effects and timings. Looks like lots of FAQ to come.

Player movement causing exploration is an invitation to being hit first by monsters even before you can retaliate.

Monster movement defines tiles....so where do they land? Which Square? its immportant as this landing-square causes other players to determine where they can or cannot go.

As some plus points. Miniatures are reasonably nice. Losy plastics used. Fast combat resolution. Few-er cards than standard dungeon crawl games, as there are no weapons/armour/spell cards besides those you start with.

Overall, a simple game, badly supported by the rule booklet. Can never be on the same level as Descent. Lacked excitement playing the game. Plain average, nearer to plain boring.

RATING: 8
This is by far the best of the 'intro to D&D' boardgames that have come out over the years. I know, not really saying much since most of those were pretty bad but it's pretty clear that's the market WotC has aimed this product at. This is not a RPG however. It is also not an in-depth dungeon crawl either so it's not to be compared to Descent or other similar games with more depth. 

The game is a light, team based tactical combat game set in the D&D 4e world. The rules are pretty simple and easy to understand. I've seen a lot of complaining about the rules and I really just don't understand where it all comes from. I can only imagine it comes from those that are used to having every little detail spelled out for them. The rules are designed to be light and quick to pick up. Just keep in mind that if it doesn't say you can't do it then it's allowed. In keeping with the light nature of the game, the playing time is fairly short. The hour listed on the box is pushing it a bit though. It does depend on the number of characters you have in play. I'd say on average you'll finish in 1 hr. + 15 minutes per character. 

The components are excellent. All the minis are unpainted sculpts from the D&D 4e minis line. The tiles and counters are all thick cardboard similar to what you get in Descent. The cards are nice. Nothing incredible but what I would expect from a game like this. And everything fits back in the box nicely. The insert is one of the better ones I've seen recently and easily accommodates everything that comes with the game. Just be sure to NOT throw out the cardboard backing after you punch the game. You need to save some of those to raise the storage insert up to the lip of the box bottom. This is nicely explained in the rules. 

There are 13 adventures in the game. But unlike similar games there is a good deal of replayability in each adventure. Because the map tiles are random as are the monster, treasure and encounter cards you can get wildly varying play with the same adventure. Each character also has customizable abilities or powers. Abilities which might work in one adventure might not work as well in others so the selection of these powers is important. I've been challenged with each adventure. The tactical situations are best described as puzzles that the players have to solve as a group. The monsters and villains are well tested since they are pulled from D&D 4e. The programable nature of the monsters works very well and it is fun to puzzle through how to defeat them. 

Overall, I think this is a solid game for a lighter dungeon crawl. The rules are short and easily explainable. And the playing time is fairly short for this type of game. The game also is great for families. The age on the box is 12+ and I can see how that might be the case if a group of kids were to play. But when played with a parent the game could easily accommodate younger kids. In that case I'd say you could easily get kids in the 8-10 yr. old range to play with a good level of autonomy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I really enjoy dungeon crawlers as a genre. I had high hopes for a Dungeons and Dragons themed game even though all my experience with Wizard's published games screamed "don't get your hopes up!". I should've listened to my instincts. Don't get me wrong, there's definitely a diamond in this very very rough, but it would require repurposing many of the components for a much better custom game. And speaking of the components...

I'm a tactile person. I enjoy having nice pieces (minis, where appropriate, are a plus!). But Castle Ravenloft's graphical design is just bland and boring. I literally could not prevent myself from yawning every time I saw the cards and tiles in the game. Each of them seem to be designed like bland corporate brochures with one color, up to two flat shades of that color, and white+black. That's it. The "art" on the cards consists of monochrome sketches. Instead of eye-grabbing icons for things like "Immobilized", instead we're treated to just the word itself with really badly done generic token backgrounds. I can't emphasize enough how every tile and card is just a generic texture plus a word. It felt like I was playing a prototype for an unpublished game right down to the rules that clearly need tweaking. On the other hand, the minis are excellent. 

The game's actual rules fall really flat. Most of the decisions they made don't make any sense in the context of the game and it slowly reveals how untested it feels. Most of the time, half the party will be stuck on level 1 for the entire adventure due to a really stupid rule that requires rolling a nat 20 (which you only really do in combat) by that character while you have 5 experience. Afterward, you can't ever level again. That doesn't feel very D&D at all. That's just like if someone knew the general premise of D&D but couldn't be bothered to balance the game properly. It keeps all the boring parts of the TTRPG: Armor class, healing surges, daily powers, a single level. Without adding any of the good stuff.

Each adventure feels almost the exact same, even moreso than Descent 2e did. Move onto tile, fight whatever's there, reveal monster/encounter, get suckerpunched (EVERY GODDAMN TURN) and repeat for EACH player's turn. I understand this was to scale the game's difficulty by player but it really means there's no teamwork. Every player just hits whatever's closest to them and splitting up actually stops the party from dying as fast since too many encounters hit every hero on one tile. It's seriously the best and only way to play. After you learn that extremely basic tactic, the only remaining gameplay feature is the pure ass-chapping randomness. Dungeon crawlers need to be random because it adds variance and replayability. Castle Ravenloft on the other hand will eat up all that variance in one adventure. The game is just too random and you experience a slew of never-ending bullshit on a constant basis. We won our adventures but it wasn't very fun or strategic. It was the same tactic of figuring out how to get close and rip into 1-3 HP as fast as possible every turn. The fact that monsters move based on hero turns was very confusing and difficult to keep track of once things got moving. Multiple times, that rule felt like it was written incorrectly and slowed the game down tremendously. There's better ways to balance based on how many players are in the game.

And the SUCKER PUNCHING. Jesus christ not a single monster we encountered just spawns and sets something up. Every single one of them spawns, moves an entire tile to a hero, and slams them with their best attack instantly. You'd think heroes would learn eventually to guard after an encounter.

Overall, this game plays like it was designed by a corporate publisher: soulless, vapid, shallow, and yawn-inducing. Only reserve for game nights where you have 45 minutes to kill and want to go to bed after.

RATING: 4
Customisation - lots of options available through official and community created content including: plethora of scenarios, custom campaigns with new heroes and even changed gameplay mechanics to include proper leveling. Now I'm not someone who wants to customise their game (outside of sleeves and plano boxes) as I prefer to play vanilla or with official expansions, so I attribute this as the 2nd biggest reason I'm getting rid of them. If you love customising games and using lots of variants with PnP stuff then this may be for you!

Components - Top notch, card stock is good along with the quality of the tiles, love how they lock into each other. The insert is actually quite usable even though I opted for deck boxes and planos  You'll be getting your monies worth in terms of box contents.

Minis! - This gets it's own category! When I first started collecting boardgames I originally wanted games with Mini's and games like Mage Knight seemed so abstract and hard to grasp with it's coloured tokens. I also had plans to paint them which lasted for about 10mins after I got them home. Now that I owned and have played a few more games I've noticed that a lot of games substitute mini's to hide shallow mechanics and unfortunately I feel these games are that, plus I have no issues anymore believing my purple wooden token is a dog! cool

Gameplay - I love complex games but also really simple games like PAGC, Elder Sign and Dungeon Roll... But the D&D Adventure Games were just a chore to play, for the setup time and length of gameplay I really felt like I was just going through the motions to finish the game in order to say I'd done another playthrough and I tend to enjoy longer games like Robinson Crusoe and Mage Knight. Rolling a single D20 is also a bit of a bummer and I like ideas where people have instead used 2x D10's.
Mechanics are too predictable (i.e. A monster WILL spawn if you encounter a new tile) and takes away immersion from the game, games like Galaxy Defenders and Gears of War have similar mechanics but still maintain some unknown elements in them that keep the game fresh and fun (I don't own either but have played them). I also thought about keeping this game to play with my wife/friends but even they were bored with the game after a very short time.

Theme - This was the biggest reason for me to ditch them! For me as Theme can make or break a game for me in terms of creativity and imagination. I had real troubles getting immersed in these games and letting my creativity take over. I felt the theme just wasn't there or at least kept pulling me out, aside from the mini's everything else just seemed... lifeless. 
The tiles themselves are good but all very samey with not too much difference. The cards were the worst bit, very little art or style, the font was bad and I haven't seen many people comment about it but for me the font was the biggest immersion breaker. I'm not looking for cursive text or an aged scroll to read off but at least choose something less... sterile and office like. Theme is one of the main reasons I enjoy Mice and Mystics, if the theme wasn't there on that game I'd probably have the same opinion as I have here. Some of the tokens were also just a coloured background with plain font text saying what it was which just seemed lazy if they were able to print proper art on other tokens. 

Support - I really felt WoTC let the whole team down on this one, they virtually copy pasted their games after Ravenloft

RATING: 3
So far, I don't find it really works either in gameplay or theme terms. There is not really much in the way of interesting strategy or tactics. That wouldn't matter to me if the gameplay meshed well with an immersive theme. And it's almost there: really nice miniatures and so on. But the game pushes you towards exploring fast, even when thematically that seems wrong. It pushes you towards keeping your party spaced out. You have to linger by an unexplored area and, every time, you are pounced on by a monster who surprises you. The lovely monster miniatures aren't usually the biggest threat, rather the encounter cards. There isn't enough variety in the rooms or what happens when you explore. I'd prefer more variety between character classes. I really think a few changes to the rules could rescue this, but I'm not sure if I'm going to get round to trying this out.

Traded away for ¬£25 in Maths trade.

RATING: 2
Gorgeous component, enormous potential, and totally failed game. Boring to death, crippled, and again boring. 
How can a game with such potential be so bad? That i am still trying to understand.

Why are those wonderful tokens being unused? Why are those gorgeous monsters all the same? Why are those AI cards all identical (rush and hurt)? Why are those "treasures" almost feel as a punitive measure? Why is the turn so more scripted? Why are those tiles all completely useless? There's a grid but monsters move by TILES. What horrible idea is that? 
So much questions...

Basic turn: you move, you hit the monster (usually, there's only one monster on the board at a time), you chose to discover and get hit by a random encounter or be hit by a random encounter. Then you turn stop. You get hit by the new monster that appears exactly the same place on the new tile, it hits you without any way to prevent it, and then it's the other player turn. Then you move, you hit, and you discover. You get hit by the event, hit by the monster, and then you move......

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Who wouldn't want to take on the likes of Count von Strad?  Well, this game is your chance... sort of.  In actual play, the game is a clever, if not a bit simple, dungeon crawl, but it will not take you back to your AD&D days of Castle Ravenloft; nor will it bring a flood of role-playing back to you.  What it will provide is a mostly straight forward system in which you may experiment with the D&D 4E combat system and with a bit of attention and effort could relate an interesting tale of adventure.  The component quality is solid as well.  So it has some strengths.  

It also has some negatives.  The original as printed rules are not particularly good and in some ways are pretty down-right poor.  The revised rules available on the WotC website are much improved and essential - but not everyone knows to go hunt them down.  Experienced gamers - sure; but the bulk of the target market for the game?  Not as likely perhaps.  The programmed monsters work, but are frankly a bit simplistic in their tactics.  and its a bit surprising that more character development is possible - nor is a campaign available out of the box.  This factors all add up to down grade the game a notch or two.

So if you combine the good and bad, you get a respectable mid performing game that really has quite a bit of potential... if it's ever exploited in future releases or enhancements.

RATING: 4
After three plays, I can say with some certainty that this game fails at what it attempts to be.  The crisis management aspect is frustrating, as you nearly always lose hit points every round to attacks you can't avoid.  Being cautious punishes you with events, but being bold punishes you with monster synergy.  Sometimes the events slap you for sticking together, sometimes for spreading out; there is no way to know which it will be.

The game does not sell the horror theme at all.  It feels more like running a gauntlet: plow through to the finish line before the constant beatings knock you out.

It doesn't feel like a dungeon crawl, either.  The placement and orientation of the tiles had little to no impact on the gameplay, and the monster AI is purposefully stupid so the heroes have a chance.

There are better crisis management games, there are better horror games, and there are better dungeon crawlers.  Fail.

RATING: 8.6
This board game is great to tear you away from World of Warcraft for a few hours.
It's a dungeon crawler with excellent mats and gameplay.

The plus: the system adapts itself to the number of players (1-5) and the players all play against the system.

While it may be not as surprising as a player based dungeon master, the design is marvellous and it is a real dungeon crawler. 

The game is a board game of the D&D system, but developped without much pretention which is great.

I love it since the time to play it is between 1 hour and 1hr 15.

Quite hard to achieve victory. Around 20% win / 80% loss if not cheating of course :))

Update 06/2011: game is a blast. For those wanting a fast dungeon crawler with fun gameplay and tense actions.

These days I mostly play it solo with 2 heroes: if you don't take a healer, it is a blast and a challenge to beat the scenarios.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I initially rated this game highly -- the concept of DM-less gameplay excited me, and the D&D branding electrified the air.

I played my first game, which went well for the 3 of us, but I definitely felt a tinge of "Is that it?".  My second game was a narrow victory, and that felt great -- surviving by the skin of your teeth, whew, there's nothing like it.  Since then, however, I've lost every game I've played.  Who likes to play a game that they always lose?  In this case, I don't.  

I like the idea of CR, but most often it boils down to "Do I really want to spend the next 2 hours doing repetitive actions ... ultimately to just lose the game?"  

Beyond this basic, perhaps superficial reason to judge a game harshly, I think that the game play is much too repetitive.   By the basic "monsters always attack first" mechanic and the "encounters nearly all kick your tail regardless of what you do" problem, it would require a person an extreme degree of luck to actually beat most scenarios.

I just spent some time reading other low posts for this game, and many others (in the 2-3 range) have elaborated on the things I've mentioned.  For anyone interested in this game, be ware.  I'm hopeful that future expansions will fix the problems with this game, but I recommend that only after that point do you drop the cash to purchase this game. bg

RATING: 8
A decent dungeon crawler, Castle Ravenloft suffers from some mechanical issues, the main being how the monsters on a newly explored tile behave: there‚Äôs no way you can prevent those creatures from attacking you or a companion, let alone have a chance at defeating it before it strikes, which means you need to think twice whether to push deeper into the dungeon or wait for another player to do it.

Decidedly this suits the theme perfectly (it simulates conditions such as limited visibility and ambushes) but just seems odd: it really feels like a cheap ploy to let the monsters attack the party at least once, thus making the game quite hard.

On this subject, there‚Äôs no doubt Castle Ravenloft is deadly: most creatures have high attack bonuses and hit often; the encounter cards themselves, which you draw more often than you‚Äôd wish, are very dangerous, as no card in the deck is helpful to the party; and most of the treasures aren‚Äôt that powerful compared to the dangers the players have to face in the game.
All in all, two curative surges are often way too few to storm the dungeon successfully.

On a side note, it‚Äôs funny to see how well the poor D&D 4th edition fits a boardgame, which is what I thought ever since it was published: due to most of its clumsy rules and abstract powers, D&D 4 doesn‚Äôt work as a real RPG but would make a good boardgame rulesystem.

This game just confirmed my first impressions.

RATING: 7
After several plays, I find the game pretty fun, but you need a party that is willing to take the hurt that the games dishes out.

Like many good 'crawlers, you need teamwork and careful sequencing to succeed in each scenario. You need to study your characters' special abilities and be ready to use them when the occasions arise.

Strangely, the game does no seem very cohesive from a thematic standpoint. Monsters appear and strange things happen all the time that have no great relation to one another. The game is still fun, but it's nagging that everything feels so random until you meet the boss.

So it comes down to surviving whatever is hurled at you until you meet the final baddy. Do that through a dozen or so of scenarios of increasing severity. Perhaps the best thing that can be said (until I play more) is that it's a bit like DungeonQuest with parties and dirt simple combat.

RATING: 6
Beyond the hype and the backlash, it's just a moderately fun game which gives you a Dungeons and Dragons fix in the context of a one hour board game. That said, I don't know many other games which can claim that they do that exactly. It has minor problems that probably could have been fixed - the graphic design is a little bland, for example. There are some problems that can't fixed without adding complexity and playing time. There are some issues I have which I'm unsure of - drawing Encounter cards over and over can make one a little weary. Still, you get tactics, uniquely behaving monsters and a scenario storyline in an hour. It's a solid 6 - a moderately game to bring to the table when the mood strikes you and you need that particular itch scratched. Just like that food you really have taste for one day, the game's enjoyment could on that day exceed its quality and feel like an 8 or 9.

Edit: I will confess that since purchasing it and playing it a few times, I don't often have the enthusiasm for getting this to the table with so many other great co-ops out there. I understand that this game's two sequels have expanded variety and are preferred by many gamers, but I haven't felt the need to purchase them.

RATING: 5

I've played the first few scenarios multiple times and any time we lost (after 2 hours!) we didn't have any "we should have ____ instead of ____" thoughts.

RATING: 7.5
This game is nothing special in some ways.  It has very little bling in terms of components.  The minis are nice, but the dungeon is pretty generic, and the reward cards have no illustrations.  The game feels simple.  And for some people this will be an issue; for me, though, it's the selling point.  My main problem with dungeon crawls is that they are too long, and either overwrought or repetitious.  If they are short, they are lacking in scope and strategy (a la DungeonQuest).  Ravenloft splits the difference perfectly.  You can play out a scenario in about an hour, and while it's simple, there's plenty to think about.  The reason for this is that most of the skills the characters use to control how they move and fight are limited in their use - many are one and done.  Artifacts are similar.  So when to use what you have, and how to sequence various actions amongst characters to make the best use of their skills gives you a great deal to think about.  There is a real sense of exploration, and it's fun to see how the dungeon unfolds, even if the individual parts are not so distinctive.  It's the perfect dungeon crawl for me: plays smooth and fast, but has enough meat on its bones to satisfy.  It feels thematic enough.  And it plays great solo!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Oh hey, It's D&D except it's a boardgame!

Nice for random dungeons and fun co-op. Not nice for poorly written rules. Way too many things have similar or unclear names. There are three things in the game, for example, which look like they could be "monster cards", and there is no part of the rules which actually talks about which pieces are the pieces with which names. This totally screwed up our first couple games, and we thought the came was totally terrible, until we realized that we were using the wrong 'monster cards' as the monster cards. What we were using was the 'monster placards' or something. duh, guys, come on...


It's all made better by the super sweet miniatures collection. Are you a GM who could use a hundred or so new miniatures for your tabletop battles? This set is worth picking up just for the minis. Seriously. Go look at the photos of this thing.

RATING: 4
During our first game I'm pretty sure we encountered EVERY possible rules ambiguity there in this game. What the rulebook lacks in length it makes up for in vagueness.

Also this game REALLY could have used another rules summary card with quick reminders for all the tiny rules you're likely to forget (moving diagonally is okay, except across tiles. One player can't control two identical monsters, etc.)

This game works, but that's really all there is to it. There's nothing to do besides combat, which would be fine, if the combat weren't deeply bland - there's no real interesting decisions to make here, just mostly spamming the same attack and rolling the dice every turn. The game feels like it could play itself. Yeah, it's shorter and less complex than Descent, but in this case the loss of complexity equates to loss of interesting choices and variety.

RATING: 4
I received all three of the D&D board games as a gift. While they're not bad, they're more of a dungeon crawler on fast-forward than a traditional D&D adventure. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Lightweight but very attractive dungeon crawl that provides a high-quality introductory D&D-like experience.  The rules are pretty simple though as with most board games there will be a little time required to learn them.  Once you get the hang of it the gameplay goes pretty fast.  (Rules can be summarized easily on one page -- check out the excellent summary sheet by UniversalHead.)  The adventure scenarios are well designed, even including a couple of solo adventures.  

Quality of the game pieces and the board is very nice though if you want painted minis ... that's not why you buy this game.  I personally appreciate the fact that the heros are a different color from the bad guys, which makes it much easier to see what's going on at a glance.

This one lured my 11-year-old videogame addict off the consoles to play with the family and got him asking for more.  I recommend this for families.  It's a rare cooperative game that is not simply mutual solitaire, and you can play it with kids without going brain-dead.  If you don't like the "violence" aspect -- have the weapons all be made of foam and have the monsters go off for cookies and juice when they're out of HP (srsly, take it from an experienced mom, this ruse works for sensitive kids).

I would love to see a set of "advanced" rules or an expansion set of cards for this game bringing in some additional mechanics such as skill challenges or whatnot

Update 11/14
Played scenario two and the game flowed much better and was a bit more tense, so I will bum my rating up a point. Still, the game is ok to play once in a while, but I doubt I will be buying it.

---

Nice component quality except for the really thin playing cards. There have been some complaints about a chemical smell from some copies. I have seen 3 or 4 copies now and while there was some of that smell present it was only a 1 or 2 on the Dank in Dark Stench-O-Meter. Owners with their stinky copies should just thank whichever god they believe in that it is no where near as rank as Duel. Just opening that game's box in public could cause you to get slapped with UN sanctions placed against you for suspected WMDs. Having those blue helmeted peace keepers showing up for wargame game night would be a drag!

I have only played one scenario and we jumped right into #9. There were 5 of us and it was ridiculously easy to win. I am wondering if the designers of the game were only targeting kids 10-13 and none of the "and above" crowd. We all approach the scenario logically and used strategy to funnel the monsters into kill zones. Maybe the creators thought we would just go blundering through the dungeon activating all the monsters with chaotic glee.

Also, what gives with the Blazing Skeletons" that were not cast in a translucent red plastic, but in translucent BLUE plastic. They seem to be more like Cool Mint Skeletons w/Retsyn. 

While it is nice to have a fairly quick dungeon crawl that does not require a game master I am pretty ho-hum on at least one of the scenarios. Hopefully, another game or will change my mind, but until then I only rate the game a 5.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
It is a simple cooperative hack-and-slash [u]dungeon crawler[/u] with a cinematic feeling. High thematic, very challenging, good replayability, fast paced, iconic monsters. I bought it to playing mostly [color=red]solitaire[/color] sessions (one of my best solitaire games; it has amazing scalability, so it is possible to play with just one or two characters in almost all cases!) or cooperative sessions without a Dungeon Master, and for it's wonderful components (I intend to use it's miniatures and tiles in other D&D 4e sessions). Great production value (42 minis, 42 tiles etc.).
I'm not happy with the setup time, a big space is needed to play it and the monsters move by tiles which is not very realistic. Also, it's hard to bring it to the table.
Easy to make custom scenarios for this game and a lot of free scenarios and characters/monsters can be downloaded from BGG.
UltraPro sleeved.
--I also own the sets of tiles and adventure books for the [i]Castle Ravenloft[/i] (second set), [i]Wrath of Ashardalon[/i] (incl. manual) and [i]Temple of Elemental Evil[/i] board games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
It's D&D 4e with none of the healing and lowered damage and monster hit points. On the bright side some of the character abilities are fun. On the down side it's basically a war of attrition, trying to survive and stave off damage just long enough to beat the adventure before you die. There aren't many ways to heal, and few respites from drawing monsters and/or encounters every turn, so you never feel like you're doing well.

Level-ups being random (you have to earn XP and then also roll a 20) isn't fun because it might never happen, and drastically affects the difficulty of the adventure. Having one character level up deny all other players from doing so isn't fun, because you're hurting the rest of the party who are supposed to be your friends and allies.

If you want to play D&D, just play that. A game of 4e is much more fun because you can heal and rest and change the pace of the game. If you want to play a board game there are many others with better mechanics that are more fun.

RATING: 8
After playing the first adventure with one hero, I was underwhelmed. I've really enjoyed playing later scenarios, though, with all five heroes. I love that it can be played solo. There are decisions to be made all the time, whether it's you moving and attacking or the monsters. The best I can tell, the detractors tend to dislike that the game is not very much like a simulation or not very much like role-playing (It's been years since I was a regular role-player, myself). It's more abstract than that, and if you're willing to accept the abstraction, it's a great game. There's a lot of ways to optimize your play, and the more familiar with the game you get, the more you're likely to see how you could play better. On the whole, the scenarios are very well constructed, and I'm impressed by what the designers have managed to accomplish here.

RATING: 6
A bit of a missed opportunity here.
While the game has been simplified and streamlined to get down to the 1 hour playing time, I feel they have made it a bit too simple.
There is not enough interaction and choices to be made.
Scaling betweeen number of players is great. Movement/reaction system for monsters works fine too. But the random dungeon setup simply means you will find the room you have been looking for when you have turned over all the tiles that have been randomly placed on top. So your desired location might turn up deep in the dungeon, or you might end up finding it right beside the entrance, as you turn around and explore previously ignored passages. All tiles are more or less the same, too. No special tiles with seperate rules for movement, combat etc.
As a pure hack and slash this game works. But if you want a more involved game than that, this is not the game.
I look at this game to be more of a sand box game, than anything else. The basics are solid, but it screams for house rules.
Where is the sense of adventure? Where are the hard choices you have to face together as a group?

RATING: 5
I'm enjoying this with my 8-year-old son as a fun adventure game. It's always fun to explore a dungeon and fight monsters, gain treasure, and possibly fulfill the scenario goal in the process. The game is a race, and you can't just sit and rest without consequences. But, there's a lot of randomness with all the dice rolls and card draws. Still, it's quite fun.

Some rules I wasn't clear on from reading the rules (and playing), but have since clarified:
1) Diagonal attacks are permitted for adjacency. (Use same rules for movement. i.e. diagonal for squares, but not "tiles".)
2) Shuffle all Dungeon Tiles into stack, including "special"
3) Obstacles (furniture, etc.) do not hinder movement.
4) Conditions (Slow, Immobilized) affect hero's speed, but do not affect Move actions provided by power cards. 
5) Villians are considered Monsters.
6) Players earn experience equal to the Villian's Level.

Update: Too much downtime for the boy, so he isn't interested in it. Scenarios aren't all that different, so the game is quite repetitive. Too much randomness in the treasure draws, monster draws, encounter step, and die rolls.

RATING: 7.5
Take One (Solo Play):
Hmmmm. Well, first off, I was really hoping that the "Dungeons & Dragons: Castle Ravenloft Board Game" would be less "Gauntlet: The Board Game" (I.E. like "Descent"). Dear game designers: can you possibly cobble together a dungeon crawl game that actually incorporates room-by-room surprises, mystery, traps, and problem solving instead of a constant conveyor belt of enemies to kill? Playing this I get a mental picture of four or five monsters standing around just off-board, smoking, with numbered tickets in their hands and looking at their wrist watches.

Also, I sincerely hope that the game improves strategically somewhat when played with others since I really don't feel as if I tactically had much effect on the outcome of the game. In fact, if anything, I feel as if my little plastic Dwarf friend was undone by some pretty horrendous luck.

Take Two (Two-Player Game):

The game plays SO much better with two players.  Using XP to cancel really nasty Encounters is HIGHLY recommended, since you never know when or IF you‚Äôll ever level up.  Even in forgetting to give our characters a starting Treasure card, we still made it through Scenario Two without real strife.

Take Three (Four-Player Game):
Despite the loss and the vagaries of the rules we had a blast and look forward playing it again.  The fact that the game play time is so abbreviated, it‚Äôs not so painful than say, a loss in "Descent", which kinda hurts after a three to six hour time investment.   

Scenario Three is still unconquered (Curse You, Klak!) but we completed "Daylight Assault" on our first go.

RATING: 3
OK,I'm not the greatest fan of Dungeon-crawl board games.  Most of them never reach what it feels like to play an RPG.  This one is no exception. 
Anyway.....
The positives - the miniatures are cool.  The event cards, although having minimal info, all have a flavor text line that really does well at fleshing out the encounter and explaining it at the same time. 
 
Now, the negatives.  First, the character powers are quite limited.  Second, there should be some difference between the utility and daily power - maybe something like a time track so that the daily power recycles faster/slower than the utility power.  Next, a lot of the treasure cards seemed pretty useless.  Finally, the rule book just out-and-out sucks.  It's horrid, and beneath what I would expect from WoTC.  It's almost like they really didn't care for the project, and so didn't put their best effort into it.  I'd be willing to play the game again, but it's definitely one I wouldn't waste my money buying.

OK, a second play has not changed my mind about this game. Basically, this game just isn't fun.  It's brutal, to the point that there's a much better than even chance that your character won't even be alive to complete the game.  There's no coherent narrative to really tie the whole thing together and draw you into the game.  You don't feel like part of a story like you do when playing an RPG. Bottom line, repeating myself, this just ain't FUN.  
To tell the truth, I find myself enjoying these big box FFG plasti-fests less and less.  I guess I feel that, if you're gonna play a board game, play a board game.  If you're gonna play an RPG, play an RPG.  The two just don't meet very well.

RATING: 3
Misses the whole point of DnD by not having characters persist.  Whole point of a dungeon crawl, really.  But even if you drop the DnD name off it and look at it in the context of Descent, it still falls short.  Number of monster powers and player powers aren't even close.  Pick 2 of 3?  Really?  Or pick 3 from 36.

Update 4-3: At its heart it is pure roll and move if you think about it.  No actual decisions to be made other than if the dice let you move to the next tile.  The "decision" of which power to use may appeal to the monkey audience this is targeted at.  "If there is one guy I press this button.  If there are more guys I press this big shiny button."

4e DMG has random dungeon generator where you can fight with tactics.  Road to Legend has a campaign that lets you plan character progression.  Neither are particularly good, but at least you are thinking more than rolling.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Players are dungeon heroes, cooperating below Castle Ravenloft.  Cooperative fantasy game where players take turns trying to achieve scenario objectives.  Turns are very simple - move and attack; explore the dungeon, attack with any of "your" monsters (ones you triggered).  Combat is standard D&D D20+modifiers against armour class, inflicting hit points.  The game appears to be very tough to beat, as monsters typically get first whack at you, and your hit points erode very quickly.  The difficulty can be tuned though, via different numbers of healing surges.  Nice bits, great minis, simple rules and 12 scenarios, including solitaire.  Very nice package, and fun game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This game is far from perfect and the ruleset/rulebook needs some work.  It's a shame too, really...  I think if WotC had spent another few solid months playtesting and developing this game before releasing it, they would have had an instant classic - truly worthy of all the hype.  As it stands, the system just seems incomplete to me.

All of that having been said, there are a TON of neat ideas here and the contents of the box still have enormous potential as a sandbox for making and tweaking your own dungeon designs.  There is certainly an awesome game in the box somewhere, I've no doubt about it, but the rules for it do not ship with the game.  Ashardalon (and then Drizzt) will also probably go a long way into fleshing out the system and making this the game it should have been in the first place.  Until then, it loses some points from me...

The game also loses some point for having perhaps the blandest modular board ever.  I realize that it's a dungeon - but still!  Look at Claustrophobia!  That's how beautiful modular board tiles should be!

However, the game gets bonus points for having a ridiculously large number of ridiculously awesome minis and for the system being practically designed around solitaire play.  :thumbsup:

RATING: 6
These games are fantastic especially when playing with my wife(actually all games get elevated to awesome status when playing with my wife but these ones do especially).  Get in crazy situations, blast your way out, or die trying.  I find if I burn my special abilities throughout the course of the game rather than save them for the end I have a much better time.  Great moments in board gaming have been had here.  

Ravenloft seems the most challenging/punishing of the bunch.  If you want to get whooped badly then this one is for you.

Ashardalon seems easier as a lot of the treasures are really sick (powerful).

Drizzt certainly has the most powerful characters and hence the easiest scenarios, but the characters are also the most interesting to play due to the mechanics behind their abilities.  Play without healing surges.

Ravenloft and Ashardalon heroes can be elevated to Drizzt level power with that amazaballs Ashardalon loot, although they still won't be as mechanically interesting.

RATING: 4
Though the sheer amount of card-flipping has the tendency to bog down gameplay, Castle Ravenloft manages to be sort of enjoyable despite itself.  The classes are all fun to play, and the emphasis on teamwork gives Ravenloft a feel all its own.  That said, whether you'll win or lose all boils down to luck: how many gargoyles and wraiths will turn up in your monster deck?  There is no time for rest in Ravenloft, and if you try to catch a breather, the game punishes you by tossing more monsters your way.  

In subsequent plays, I found myself having less and less fun with Ravenloft, particularly in scenarios that featured boss monsters.  Juggling the boss monsters' powers while handling random monster encounters and traps just feels overwhelming.  Believe it or not, playing *real* D&D somehow feels less complex than Ravenloft.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Playing solo is very tough. Luck is huge in this game - roll poorly and you will die and there's nothing you can do about it. Draw the wrong combination of tiles and cards and you will die and there's nothing you can do about it... except roll exceptionally well (which means consistent hits for the good guys and consistent misses for the bad guys). However, when you get multiple heroes on the board, their powers combine extremely well and you have a very fun fighting chance. The monsters are mostly undead and other night beasties (wolves, rats, spiders) so while they're thematically strong they're also the most specific of the 3 games, but they add a whole lot of welcome variety when mixed into the monster decks for the other games. (Weird note: Handling the rubbery plastic monsters during the game leaves a funny feel and smell on your fingers.)

RATING: 8.5
Nice streamlined version of D&D - not too complex and not too simplified. I enjoy this a lot and it is a game that will always see play for me. Great components and lots of them. The 5 additional scenarios on FFG website are highly recommended (2 official and the 3 competition winners).

I prefer this to WA and Drizzt due to the traditional D&D setting - you battle undead such as skeletons and zombies and the heroes are a traditional cleric, wizard, rogue, warrior and ranger.

My only dislike is the way that monsters always get to attack first - would have been cool if sometimes they don't attack the same turn you discover them.

RATING: 7

Update: I have made my peace with the design choices and I am enjoying the game for what it is - a fast playing, light dungeoncrawler that plays well solo.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
I have recently become attracted to the idea of games which offer solo-play experiences and co-operative play.  I don‚Äôt mind juggling numerous roles or simply figuring out a puzzle, as long as the game is either rich in theme or mechanically sound.  So after the enjoyment Defenders of the Realm provided I was excited by the prospect of a dungeon crawler which I could enjoy alone as well as with a group.  So I decided Ravenloft would be my next purchase.  The buzz about the game is pretty loud; so much so, that when a negative review came out the poster was ran off (ending with them deleting the post).  A game defended so passionately by such ardent fans coupled with repeated praise of the game play made Ravenloft a sure bet.  How I was wrong.
I will not go through a component, or rules breakdown as at this point it would only serve redundancy (check out the many other reviews).  What I will do is highlight the elements of Ravenloft which lead it to be what I find a vacuous game with no true decisions, and tiring repetitive game play.  
I have played through a few scenarios now, and nothing ever changes.  Ravenloft consists of a very simplified decision tree that basically runs on a very predictable autopilot for both the game system AI and for all the players.
  
MONSTERS:

Monsters are drawn every time a tile is laid.  The monster always has initiative and moves and attacks the closest hero (this never changes).  Although the monsters have wonderfully crafted miniatures to accompany their cards this is simply nothing more than aesthetic appeal.  All creatures react, and play the same.  Is there a difference?  The number of hit points one suffers.

PLAYERS:

You can attack and move, move then attack, or move twice.  However, because the AI is as such, you are most likely (with rare deviation) going to attack then move.  Rinse and repeat.
The only real decision to make is which of your powers you wish to use during your hero phase.  The powers however are all very similar, and if you have a strong one it will be saved until the final villain.  I have found it to be that players usually select the highest hitting non-exhausting power the majority of times.  To me this reduces what is really the only decision in the game to zero.
  
THEME:

This was one of the main draws for me.  Sadly Ravenloft offers very little.  The absence of significant choices, the scripted feel of all scenarios, the repetitive and predictable AI all detract from the experience.  There is no sense of exploration, no sense of sending in stronger units and holding back the frail.  No sense of developing a plan or strategy against the dark forces within the castle.  The way the AI is built, either monster movement or encounter, encourages constant movement.  It makes no sense to sit back as the result is worse, usually, than if you have to get slapped by a tile placed beasty.  

VARIETY:

With all scenarios playing out the same, all characters feeling awfully similar (powers are just tiny variations on one another), deterministic tile draws (you know when the end will appear), and no real game play decisions I find Ravenloft offers very little variety.  Therefore it also offers very little value and very little desire to be replayed.  

CONCLUSION:

Ravenloft is a major disappointment.  I have never felt so powerless over dice rolls than I do playing this game.  Usually there are decisions involved, and ways to work around rolls ‚Äì not here.  I realize the franchise tie-in has many excited but this game strips everything to the most bare bones and leaves nothing other than some cool miniatures.  Very little game play, very little theme, and very little fun. 

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Some of the boss battles are fun, but the minute complexities they add to the game really should have been implemented in more of the enemies.

Sold it.

RATING: 7
There are so many negatives to this game...and yet enough positives that keep drawing me back to playing it!

It is absolutely one of my go-to solitaire games. Probably the only dungeon crawl I have that allows me to do so. It can get pretty intense keeping track of everything when you're playing multiple characters, fighting a boss with minions, and drawing encounter cards all at the same time.

The components are a mixed bag. The minis are great. I think the tiles and cards aren't as bad as others say...they're on the plain side, sure, but when you're trying to keep track of everything that's going on, the simplicity works in it's favor.

The components also double perfectly if you want to use them for an RPG.

I would call this a sand-box game. It can be whatever you want to do with it!

Keep in mind. The rating reflects what the game IS...not so much what it could be.

RATING: 9
I think this one of the best games for those players with a wish to experiment D&D type of game.
If you are an experienced player looking for a game this one ain't for you.

I have played a few of the original scenarios with the number of players varying between 2 and 5. The game isn't difficult to learn and after that goes pretty fast. Another good thing is you don't need a dungeon master (DM) in order to play it. You'll have plenty of opportunities to interact with the rest of the players trying to figure out the best move/action etc.

The games come in a pretty neat box which allows you to store everything: cards, miniatures, board modules.
Monster miniatures are of good quality and easily identifiable. Hero miniatures are all the same color, thus a difficult to see who is who, but you'll find differences between them that help with identification. Not much of a graphical aspect to board and cards, but that didn't stop me having fun.

Of course there are things that can be improved. All in all i say this is a good game.

RATING: 8.5
I was a little unsure about this game at first as I played the first two scenarios several times with various people (they are the recommended starting scenarios after all).  I felt that the game was somewhat repetitive and favored dumb luck over strategic gameplay.  After having dug into some "new" scenario's over the last couple of days, I quickly found that while sometimes dumb luck can put a damper on things, the strategy is enhanced when you get into certain situations. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Co-op DM-less dungeon crawl action will always suck me in.
After a read through the rules, I'm not sure what all the complaints are about.
One concern for me is the quality of the cardboard; with a modular tile system such as this they will wear fast. The cards are also really thin, flimsy, and cheaply made.

With WotC having such deep pockets, and such a high price tag, I expected quality on par with FFG games. This one clearly is not, at least in the cardboard, and card department.
The quality of the minis certainly makes up for any lack of quality in the cardboard, being much better sculpted than the standard FFG fare.

The rules are simple and man does this game play quick for the genre. A coop dungeon crawl that takes less than 2 hours; simply amazing.
It could be more in depth, but in doing so, play time would thus increase. Certainly some compromises were made to reduce complexity, but it does not detract from the fun. If it's a fast and furious coop dungeon crawl you're after, then you may have just found it in this game.

Another excellent point is the fact that this game is essentially a toolkit for creating your own scenarios, which is a huge boon over nearly every other dungeon crawl out there.
Why?
Because of the random nature of the game, and the way in which the danger is mitigated, even the scenario designer will not have any advantage over one who has not played any particular scenario. Not only that, but the system allows for one to create new scenarios (with specific rules and end games) very quickly and easily; which cannot be said of Descent or it's ilk, including Warhammer Quest. Although this one feels a lot more like a puzzle to be optimally solved in a certain way, than a true dungeon crawl.

A good dungeon crawl, although lacking the character progression.

RATING: 10
I can't get over how good this game is. I've played D&D since the 1978 basic set through to 3.5 a fair few years back and several boardgames along those lines, but this is a great, fast, tense dungeon game with a beautiful and elegant rule set. It has all the D&D bits (fighters, clerics, wizards, rogues, spells, skills, feats, hit points, monsters, traps, curses, etc). It captures the feel of our typical dungeon delve, where the party's resources would gradually deplete as we ran out of healing and/or spells and it became a race to either escape or complete whatever our mission was. You can't hang around in Castle Ravenloft, but keep pushing on to get to the Big Bad or Get Whatever It Is Done.
A typical game lasts only about a dozen turns or so, easily playable in an hour. (If you think about it, that's probably only 2 minutes elapsed time for the characters!). You can even play it solo. No DM!

Also the constant drumbeat of encounter cards seems to make progress very difficult.  We had 5 players, so even with exploring new tiles we still had to draw cards at least half the time.  Are we interpreting the frequency of Encounter cards incorrectly?  We were even using all 5 healing surges, since we got wiped out so early in our first attempted scenarios (only supposed to use 2).

I'm playing with a DnD 3.5 group that's used to a lax DM, so they can run around and screw off during encounters. RATING: 7
Okay, wow. Amazing job on streamlining the dungeon crawl, guys! This game is the anti-Descent--quick, deadly, and fast-moving. The monster movement abstraction is bloody brilliant. 
A lot of the mechanics are perhaps too simplistic for my tastes, but the alternative fixes would probably make the game too cumbersome and unwieldy. As it is, this is easily the second-best dungeon crawl I've ever played (after Mage Knight Dungeons).
[EDIT] The subsequent entries in the series are vastly preferable, as they have improved both in terms of rules and (for my tastes) theming.

RATING: 8
Having only played a scenario in each, I really can't comment on whether this or Wrath of Ashardalon is better other than to say that dragons are obviously cooler than vampires. These D&D board games function as a very stripped-down version of a D&D dungeon crawl (devoid of any real role-playing). I find Descent to be a much more enjoyable experience as I think the presence of a player Overlord and the increased tactical options add a lot. However, the game length obviously suffers for it, so this is a game you can play a lot faster than Descent.

Each player gets a hero, makes some choices about starting skills and abilities, and heads into the dungeon to start exploring. Tiles are revealed from the stack, each containing a monster with its own scripted AI that it follows (generally running towards the nearest hero and trying to kill it). Combat is handled with a single roll of the d20, compared to the armor class of the target, damage value reduces target's hit points, end of turn. Each player also controls some monsters that move at the end of their turns, mainly following the AI script, but with a few player choices as well. Each game is scenario-based, so the goals and tile layouts will be a little different each time, often even within the same scenario. Working together, the players will find treasure, kill monsters, and hopefully save the day. 

The components are a mixed bag. It's an enormous box, chock-full of tiles, tokens, and plastic. However, the cards themselves, while of high quality, are very bland to look at. When you find a sword, you don't get to see a picture of it (despite the publisher's extensive library of D&D artwork they could have easily used), you get to see a bunch of text. Similarly, the plastic figures are provided unpainted, despite the fact that the publisher also manufactures painted versions. Players wanting to replace their unpainted versions with painted ones by tracking down single D&D Minis will often be halted by the high prices of some of the rarer ones. Even if it was cost prohibitive to provide these in the base game, the publisher certainly had the ability to offer a full set of painted minis separately, which would have helped out its consumers significantly. The game really comes alive using the painted minis. 

I am someone who often doesn't like co-operative games. I see them as nothing more than a group optimization puzzle, or in some cases sheer randomness with only an illusion of control. Because of this, they either lose their flavor once "solved", or simply feel like a pointless waste of time. But these games really are my favorite co-ops ever because they avoid these pitfalls. Yes it is possible to get better at the game, but this is mainly by knowing the monsters and items. And yes, randomness will determine a lot of your success or failure. But because the theme and story are so important, you don't really mind this. Ultimately, this is what D&D is all about: story over gameplay. It really works well here.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Well after reading up on this one it is [i]exactly[/i] what I expected(It pays to do homework...). I have been waiting about 8 years to play a decent DM-less dungeon game and have always wanted to play WHQ but never bit the bullet on ebay so can't compare. 

Overall I think they did a decent job here, especially considering that games can play in about an hour (which is QUITE a limitation design-wise) and it ends up being [i]fairly[/i] satisfying in the end even though lots of elements from actual D&D are really missing. I was worried about all these elements being left out but now after playing I'm not sure how much I care anymore (though I plan to add some back in...). The system feels complete the way it is mostly, for what it is, and does what it was designed to do, which is give you a basic low rules overhead and short game time dungeon game, and for what it is it's good in that regard. Note my rating is for a 2 or 3 player game, I never bother playing these kind of very low decision games with more than that. 

Call me crazy but to me anyway it almost scratched the same itch as Pandemic (probably more so Defenders but I haven't played it), that could be either good or bad for some people but I guess most people won't even get this feeling at all since mechanic-wise and theme-wise they ARE worlds apart. See I did say ALMOST and they are completely different games of course but it is a co-op game that plays in an hour where you are trying to beat the system and stop stuff from spreading (in this case monsters). Also, decisions are somewhat limited, [i]sometimes[/i] obvious and luck will often be a factor in winning or losing. So in those ways and those ways ONLY, it kind of scatches the same itch for ME, I could buy this and essentially trade away Pandemic (which I am seriously thinking about) but thats just me. I dont like to keep too many games around that do the mostly the same thing, you may not care at all and they are really completely different but I just thought I would throw that out there anyway (for the record I do like this much more but I am biased due to theme...) 

So is it a replacement for Descent?  That depends of course; for the people that can't get that to the table, yeah it could be as this is much easier to pick up and play and it gives you a dose of the same flavour. For those that want a full and rich experience, likely it won't be as this is very automated in comparison. They really do scratch different itches in that regard even though they have the same theme and that is where I was kind of going in the prior paragraph, this doesn't really feel like a true RPG adventure game and how could it playing in one hour and with no campaign rules, it does do an excellent job given that time limitation however, expectations are important here. So this is a fast and furious dungeon romp, nothing too deep (read decisions very much on the light side), EASY to pickup and play, no DM needed, and you do get the feeling of exploring a dungeon and killing off monsters for treasure. Descent is much much more elaborate and rich in its approach (though its also missing things for me) but as such not as approachable in more than one sense. Really due to their MAJOR differences they almost shouldn't even be compared, they just share the same theme. WHQ is a more apt comparison and I still haven't played (read over rules) however I do know it needs houserules to be a decent game and is much more involved than CR. To me it looks like WHQ with tweaks is better than CR (with tweaks) and CR is better than Heroquest, from my experience with HQ.  

More to that end and to summarize, I think my biggest knock on this game is its biggest strength, the automation and time to play. In order to have a richer experience the time to play would have to be extended (yes little tweaks can improve this game and stay in one hour but I want more than that as do many others), more rules would have to be added and you would need someone playing the bad guys (though the last point is debatable...). OTOH, a game like that already exists but goes on too long for most people (even me mostly though RTL helps) and running into 4 hours or more means you might as well play actual D&D in many cases. So in the end I'm kind of torn on this one for now, houserules and variants could really add to the game which means to me there is STILL a BIG hole in the genre for the middle ground (a 2 hour rich experience is very possible). I like this game for sure, its good at what it sets out to do, a fast DM-less dungeon romp that you could even play with your spouse or kids but at the same time it feels like the game kind of plays itself (another Pandemic parallel) to some degree (which of course it has to to a large extent with no DM) and it lacks some overall variation and richness which makes it a little too predictable and repetitive leaving you wanting something more (a monster on every tile is anticlimatic etc, I plan on tweaking things like this though and I here some of the scenarios up this ante a bit). The game mostly did keep me interested while playing (let's see how I feel in game 5, might be kind of samey), combat was simplified but alright, items/treasure were interesting but lacking somewhat and it's a great value for 50 bucks but I do wonder how long this title could keep my interest and this is where fan-made stuff [i]might really[/i] help (and I know I'll be coming up with my own ideas too and adding them). Also note, I think games like Prophecy, Runebound, Spacehulk etc just aren't really my thing, they leave me wanting more. If you're not willing to tweak the game, becareful what you expect out of a one hour co-op D&D game; how much can really happen in a game that plays to completion in an hour.... 

[u]UPDATE:[/u] - Have not played WHQ but read over the rules and looked through the roleplaying and adventure book. WHQ is the real deal, it needs a few tweaks but looks very very good. CR is actually a boiled down version, there are several definite similarities and CR basically took [i]some[/i] of the great parts, simplified them, threw in some 4E stuff and made a game from that which plays in an hour. For some people, this will be the cat's meow, an hour very easy to play crawler is a great thing. For others who want more and still don't want a DM, WHQ is where to get it.   

[u]UPDATE:[/u] I still think Warhammer Quest will be much better than this is but I played a game of this the other day with quite a few small rule changes and it turned out quite enjoyable and MUCH better than the rules as written which are dull and repetitive in comparison. Putting some tension and intrigue back in the game makes a great difference in feel, I think this will save the game for me (from the trade pile). I'll post them soon, it shares some of the ideas that other variants posted have but also introduces a few new things as well and is very simple to implement overall. Rating with tweaks 7 or 7.5

=> Traded (acquired WHQ finally, read rules much better)

RATING: 7
This is a "dungeon crawl" style game where the "dungeon" isn't really the kind that most think of, relatively empty with a few monsters and some treasure waiting for the players to find. This is an armed fortress of a castle owned by one of the really big baddies from the D&D universe, the vampire Strahd. Character's entering the dungeons beneath Strahd's castle should expect him to put up a ton of resistance... and he does.

Going with this, the game is extremely harsh and players should not expect to win easily. A bad run of encounter or monster cards has the potential to be absolutely devastating. This can be extremely frustrating and may be too much for some players.

That said, this difficulty also makes the game incredibly tense (a good thing) and successfully finishing a game by overcoming particularly difficult challenges gives a huge sense of accomplishment. Games are also relatively short to play at about an hour per game, so re-playing after a particularly disastrous run is very easy to do.

The game is also immensely re-playable with no two games being the same, even two games of the same scenario. The dungeon is created and stocked randomly giving a very "rogue-like" feel to the game.

Overall I think this is a really excellent game and can see myself playing this for a long time to come. Possible classic here.

RATING: 8
UPDATE: I have sold this because I played it a lot and I like the updated mechanics in Drizzt. I think this game is probably better than Drizzt for newcomers, due largely to better scenarios.

This is not a deep game but it does have lots of tactical decision making. This is standard fare for dungeon crawl games which I love so this doesn't bother me at all.

There are several very cool mechanics in this game. The automated dungeon is very well done with monster and environment cards popping up to offer challenging opposition.

The scenarios are diverse and the difficulty ramps up considerably. I don't think I've won the final conflict yet.

There some reasons why people might not like this game. It does suffer from Alpha-gamer syndrome, and even without that many decisions feel like they are being made by committee. The D20 and card draws add a fair bit of luck to the system.

RATING: 4
This game is frustrating. Not fun. But leaves you thinking that with a few changes it may work. And you can actually find many fan-made variants online (including this site). Despite this imperious need to be "fixed", the game has some mechanics that I like.

For example, I really like the mechanics for monster generation and activation. Basically, monsters appear by drawing "monster cards". The player who found the monster takes its card, and that monster's turn always takes place after the player's turn. It really creates nice alternations of turns between heroes and monsters that reminds me of DnD combat.

Overall the game does a good job giving that DnD feeling. However, there's a couple of things that go directly against that. Whenever a hero doesn't "explore" a new tile, the player draws an "encounter card". Those cards have negative effects that usually affect all heroes in the same tile where the active hero is. This causes to be safer to split the party than to advance into the dungeon as a group (DON'T SPLIT THE PARTY!!). Another big thing going against the spirit of DnD is that there is no "campaign mode" that allows you to level up and better equip your character in the course of several games.

I find plenty of issues with the art in this game. First, although the sculpts of the miniatures are generally good, their scale (especially the humanoid ones) differs between heroes, monsters and villains. And Kavan, represented by a token, could have been represented by a miniature as well. Second, the back side of encounter, treasure and monster cards are plain and uninspiring, and there are no illustrations even for items, environments or traps anywhere. And third, there is way too much unnecessary cardboard... I'll give some examples:

- Condition markers could have been printed both sides as Slowed/Immobilized markers. The same goes for Reaction tokens (Calm/Enrage).
- HP and Monster HP tokens could be the same, so you don't need two pools. Alternatively, the HP of the heroes could be tracked on top of their Hero card using only one token per hero.
- To have monster cards and monster tokens seems redundant to me, the designers should have just pick one mechanic and save paper or cardboard.
- Plenty of item tokens are only used once. Those tokens could have been easily showing numbers that one assign to specific items or whatever one wants.

Besides ecological aspects, all that cardboard only makes the game more expensive, gives a false impression of content and occupies space.

In summary, the game is a frustrating cooperative DnD-inspired fight-oriented random-generated ameritrash dungeon-crawler for kids.

As it stands, the rules feel strange and disjointed, almost as if they kept removing rules trying to make the game simpler and ended up going a bit too far. RATING: 4
Walk down a grey corridor while an unimaginative card deck kills you randomly.

The "D&D Adventuring System" is:
1. One monster and one treasure per tile.
2. Random traps.
3. Random Environment
4. Random loss of hit points
5. Nothing else

The miniatures are nice, and I like the HP counters.
I came close to putting this in the garbage can.

In each quest, the objective tile is shuffled into a random location near the end. If it is near the beginning, you win, if it is at the end you lose.

RATING: 6.25
Ever watch an archer take careful aim, let loose the arrow, and the arrow follows a graceful arc right into the exact center of the haystack next to the target? That's Castle Ravenloft.

This has many elements of what could be a nice dungeon crawl adventure game, except for, well, the gameplay.

Let me review my choices for this turn. Well, I could take a swing at a monster...oh, there isn't actually any reason ever why I wouldn't take a swing at a monster. Okay, I could try to explore the next tile...oh, there isn't any reason why I wouldn't explore a tile if I could. Well, let's see what's on the tile, there might be a monster...oh, there's always a monster.

There are no choices in this game and the tiles are all the same. It only gets rated this highly because I can run through solo adventures, if you can really call them that.

RATING: 6
While several bad die rolls can really harm you and the graphical design of the cards aren't the best, this game has a ton of minis to really immerse you into the game, and there are lots of fanmade adventures to keep you busy. While the tiles are bit thinner than my liking don't have that much variety between them, that still did not detract from the overall feel that you were actually exploring through a dungeon, and you are punished for taking your time. Also, I'm not quite sure what it is about this game (maybe it's just my individual group) but alpha game syndrome seemed much less in this game than any other co-op I've played. Rules are extremely vague about a ton of things though.

EDIT: After about 15 plays the beginning slog of random monsters is starting to feel really samey,and I wish there was more differentiation between adventures. It doesn't help that the variety of monster types is limited by the minis.

RATING: 6
An excellent cooperative game where players assume the role of D&D adventure archetypes.  This a DM-less game where you explore a dungeon and fight monsters and work to complete various scenarios.  Each challenge is different.

This is an excellent game and it is very good at 4 or 5 players.  2 or 3 just does not work well.  You are much more likely to lose at lower player counts on most scenarios.  You should just have a couple of players play 1 or 2 characters.  

This may be true for Ravenloft with the low player counts.  Other versions of the game may not have the issue. 

Even with this problem, I still like the game a lot.

RATING: 3
Unless we are missing a crucial rule, my husband and I agree that the turn structure makes this game way too hard to be enjoyable. Movement/Action > Exploration > Villain means that you can never attack a monster on a turn it appears but it can always attack you. You also constantly dead-end yourself because exploration of a new tile can take place ONLY after you have finished all movement for the turn, so every monster you managed to leave behind you can easily catch up in a turn or two, and any monster on a newly revealed tile can attack you immediately. And, as if you actually needed an incentive to explore a new tile every turn, if you don't do so, you have to draw an encounter card that will add a trap or damaging condition to the tile you are on. So if you don't explore, the bad guys catch up with you and you add even more danger to your location. If you do explore a tile, the bad guys catch up with you, and you add a new bad guy to the board that gets to attack you right away while you can't do anything about it. The leveling up system is weird and stunted (you can only do it if you roll a 20!). While I understand this is a cooperative game, I also don't like the shared experience pool mechanic.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
I don't enjoy playing this game. At the risk of repeating all of the other criticisms out there, here are my thoughts...

The biggest problem is the rules don't feel like give the players any choices or control at all -- you either explore a new tile and trigger a monster  and possible an encounter, or you trigger an encounter (even if you're actively engaged in combat.) The monsters and encounters often include unavoidable damage. Most powers become exhausted after a single use and don't reset until you  manage to get one of the very few dedicated cards that reset them (even resetting on a natural 20 would feel more balanced and exciting.) This means that you end up choosing between your at-will powers for almost the entire game. And to make it worse, most of the powers don't have enough of an effect to merit their use. For example, one power allows you to push back a monster, but this doesn't in any way limit the monster's ability to attack you when it activates in almost all cases. So you might have well just used the at will power with the highest bonus to hit.

Speaking of bonuses, the monsters power seems quite arbitrary. Kobold skirmishers have +9, which means they hit the most heavily armored character on a 6 or something close to that. Really strange.

Any game I've played without the Cleric feels like punishment as opposed to a game. The items are unremarkable and often come with rules or limitations that make them feel weak.

The characters and their abilities are ripped right from the pages of D&D source material, so that shouldn't disappoint.

The quality of the components varies. For instance, the encounter, treasure, and item cards are terribly bland with no art. The monster cards have some art and are ok. The highlight being the interlocking room tiles and the miniatures which are nice enough to justify the purchase if you can get it for under forty bones, which shouldn't be too difficult.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

A game that feels like it was designed with a focus on game mechanics, and then stapled on with a theme. I feel that the need to make a fantasy RPG game available for both solo and group gamers, and playable in under an hour kills it for our gaming group.
