=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I should start off by saying that I am not a fan of D&D, and never really played the RPG, but I do appreciate D&D for what its done for the RPG genre.  I do like RPGs in general, and dungeon crawls especially. 

Having said that, D&D:CR is a really fun co-op game, and quite different from the D&D RPG system (or at least the previous editions).  The game does have some flaws, but it is still a fun experience.  

The treasure cards aren't that great as most of them are useless (depending on the situation), and the game could've used more unique \ special tiles.  As is, most of the tiles are identical, and serve no special purpose.  A more in-depth leveling system could have also been nice.

I do, however, use coffin markers in all my games \ scenarios.  It makes some of the tiles at least a bit more fun \ different, and adds a bit to the game.

However, despite a few flaws, I think the game is a lot of fun.  Most dungeon-crawl games require a DM (Dungeon Master of some sort).  Since my gaming group is limited, that means I usually end up as the DM, which means I have to fight against my friends \ wife.  I always wanted a game that I could play with them, on the same side, and now I can!

I usually like more complex games, and I do agree that D&D:CR is rather simple, but it's the simplicity that makes this game work.  I am usually limited on time, so I rarely get to play the large epic games, like Descent.  We can easily play a game, or two, in a couple of hours.

As the rule system is simple, the game is very easy to pick up, and teach to others.  Most of the time I don't even really have to teach the rules, just get right into the game, which is nice.  Each scenario is also unique, and fun, so there is always something new to try.  Once you tried everything, you can always design a new scenario.

I should also mention that the figures \ models look amazing.  Yes, they are unpainted (most games are anyway), but they still look great.  I am a big fan of nice looking games \ components, and this game does not disappoint.  Some artwork on the cards would have been nice, but I am happy with what we got; the system is very streamlined.

The game can also be challenging at times, and may seem like you are constantly getting beat down, but I think that makes the game more fun.  So far we haven't lost yet, but we came really really close each time.  This makes the game more exciting, as you are always on the edge.  You can also tweak the difficulty a bit, quite easily, simply by adding an extra healing surge token.

Some people say the game does not give the players enough tactical choice.  For instance, you will almost always get hit by a monster 1st, with no way to react.  While this may seem unfair, it makes the game work.  There are plenty of house rules out there to circumvent that, and I tried out my own system as well.

While the choices may not be as diverse \ broad as in some other games, you still have to make decisions that do affect the outcome.  Choosing the right powers, for the right characters, to create a synergy between the group is very important.  

Also, since most of the really powerful abilities can only be used once per game (for the most part), the players have to be really careful when to use them, and how.  This usually creates some good discussions amongst my gaming group, as I like to analyze my choices, and come up with the best one.

Lastly I will also mention that there are more D&D games planned for down the line, that should add more variety to the system.  D&D: Wrath of Ashardalon just came out, and from what I have heard it is an improvement over D&D:CR.  However, since the tiles, heroes, monsters, and other cards are different, I think people that like this system should pick up D&D:CR as well, as it will give you more choices \ variety to integrate into your games (I am also a fan of the undead theme).

Overall, I think this game is really fun, one of the very few games out there that can be played co-op without a need for a DM, and can also be played solo!  The theme is also nice, and the components are great.  I would recommend it to most dungeon crawl fans, and people with limited gaming time, and a small gaming group.

P.S 

I play with a few house rules.  With said house rules, I would rate the game close to a 9.0 for the fact that it is easy to teach and play.  You get a good dungeon crawling fix for less effort \ time than some other games in this category.

I recommend an initiative variant and extra rules for special tiles (for instance some type of item check for the laboratory, etc.).

RATING: 5
Still can't point out what irritates me about this game. I guess maybe how simple the game is (I liked Descent a lot more even with how long it is).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
I haven't played DnD in a long time, but I really enjoyed games like Descent, Runebound, and Dungeon Quest.  I was looking for a lighter dungeon crawl that didn't take so much setup time, and based on the exceptionally high rating on BGG, I thought I'd pick up DnD: CR.
Upon reading through the rulebook, I was very confused about how certain aspects of the game work.  It seemed very light on describing things like creature attacks and movement, but alas, I dived into my first game with my girlfriend, who also has enjoyed similar games, but has never played DnD.
We both found this game horribly boring and uninspired.  The cards are all bland and don't make sense.  Every time I drew a treasure for a monster I was victorious over, it seemed more like a trap or penalty most the time.  Uggh!  
We were constantly in the rulebook, which is silly, since it's so short to begin with.  You'd think "why am I in here so much, there are hardly any rules?", but the reality is, so many things are very unclear and so simplistic that they barely make sense.
We finished out our game, but we were bored to death, and actively tried to kill ourself by spawing more monsters.  Honestly, this was more fun than drawin encounter cards and getting yet another trap you can't defend against.
In the end, the game beat us before the game ending tile came up.  
All this game made me want to do was play another game of Thunderstone, or dust off Runebound and play a game with some flare, or a decent combat system.
It really seems like WotC is trying to find an easily accesible game for DnD, but this surely cannot be it.  If you want a game with depth and dungeon crawling, get Descent, if you want something lighter, grab Runebound or possibly the new Dungeon Quest.  Much more interesting games that have flare and inspiration.  
Sorry DnD: CR....you're going on Ebay or into the discard pile.

Good:
-Setup is super quick.
-Nice minis.
-Easy combat resolution.
-Interesting dungeon placement system.
-Monster movement system if fairly interesting.

Bad:
-Abundance of punch out pieces that seem to have no use.  (Apparently they are used for other adventures?)
-Terribly boring cards.  Washed out monster cards with only a pic of the mini.  No color.  Treasure cards have no pictures or flare.  Also washed out and a single color.
-Heroes seems very lackluster and uninspired.  
-Hero abilities are very limited and boring.
-Drawing treasure seems like a 50/50 between being good or actually being a penalty.  Isn't an encounter where the penalty should lie?
-No effective way to restore life, other than possibly always have someone be a cleric and pass turns to restore health, and praying an encounter doesn't kill you first.
-Awful rulebook with errors in text and very limited examples.  There isn't a single example of how a monster moves from tile to tile to attack you.  FYI, most can just launch themselves at you from tiles away for considerable damage. Huh?
-Still confused as to what dungeon tiles I include in the stack.  No rulebook information on what happens if you can't lay a tile due to corridor issues.
-Component storage is really out of wack.  Seems like WoTC wants me to punch all the dungeon tile "back in" for easy storage?  

I'm really not sure how everyone thinks this is so great.  Maybe most play a lot of DnD, and they just want something simpler, but I'm not sure this is it.  Follow my above reccomendations if you want a better dungeon crawl experience.

Thanks!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Putting the words "Dungeons and Dragons" in the title means an automatic demerit from me, because D&D is absolutely the worst roleplaying game I've ever played.

Game play of Ravenloft (and by extension, I assume, the others in the series) is pretty boring, to be honest, though not exactly terrible. It's got the usual random monster assortment, simple rules and dungeoncrawlyness you'd expect. But it's just a bit blah, and despite the opportunities for cooperative play, there's not much of a narrative. It's more of a an "Oh, look, two more monsters materialized out of thin air, let's chop them into little pieces" sort of thing. I'm actually okay with that. I mean, the whole idea that all these monsters live within inches of each other 24 hours a day and don't eat, poop, move or even fight each other is a standard fantasy trope. But you better handle that in an interesting, or at least fun way, or I'm just going to point and laugh.

Claustrophobia is much better, is thematically stronger and takes less time. There's nothing to Ravenloft that would ever make me play it instead.

RATING: 8
It fills a niche for an older D&D gamer like myself. I've been dying to play D&D again, but being a DM seems silly at my age unless playing with kids and nobody wants to be the bad guy against everybody else. A great fast paced game full of strategy. Lots of important decisions on every turn. Where you end your movement, how to deal with monsters, traps, villains and encounters and not wanting that 4hp elf in your party to explore is just part of the fun. 
It is a team game, you really have to work as a party and there is so much going on in each players area, it is impossible for anybody to take somebody else's turn. Generally you know what needs to be done on your turn anyway (some good ol' monster killin.)
 My fav part of this game is EASILY being able to run a campaign, come up with your own story with actual plot, character dialogue and such without having to change a thing about the game.

RATING: 8
This is one that will appeal to those of us that have grown up playing D&D - obvious really! The mechanics work really well and for a dungeon crawl it is truly great! The scenario book gives what appears a easy starting adventures, but by scenario 3 the forces of evil aren't quite such a push-over.
The game is very much fight after fight with minimal scene setting, which is the biggest difference from playing D&D with a human DM. But if that doesn't bother you then here is a really tremendous co-operative game, which I think provides an opportunity for many expansions (even home written ones)!
What more could you ask of a game that plays in an hour!
HIGHLY RECOMMENDED.

RATING: 5
While I want to love it, Castle Ravenloft has a problem with pacing. You're punished for not rushing into the dungeon, opening all the doors and alerting all the denizens ASAP, there are deadly traps everywhere and it is isn't, thematically speaking, the weekly bad thing in Pandemic or Shadows over Camelot. No, it feels like stepping in the poop every other second.

Aside from that, the core mechanic, "interpreting and manipulating the AI of the monsters so they position themselves at the optimal spot for your group to deal with them", doesn't feel satisfying and a bit too "meta" for a game that has the pretention of being an immersive dungeon crawler with great minis and thick cardboard.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Really liked this at first, but it started to get very repetitive after a few plays.  The movement system is a bit fiddly.  Hero movement and adjacency is by squares and monster movement and adjacency is by tile.  This leads to some oddness where the hero can melee a monster that can't melee back.  I feel like they should just have simplified it all the way and had heroes move and count adjacency by tile as well.  I also would have preferred less events and more monsters.  Fighting monsters is more interesting than resolving a random event card.  Also, there are only five different characters and they don't really have a unique feel.

RATING: 7
In this cooperative dungeon crawl game set in the D&D universe, players choose between a cleric, ranger, fighter, wizard or rogue. On a player's turn they move/attack and explore new dungeon tiles, which turn up monsters. Combat is resolved in the D&D way, rolling a 20 sided die and resolving against armor class. 

The games are fun, full of tension, and engaging. Looking forward to going through all the 13 adventures.

Update:

Okay, so we've almost played through the entire book and my initial optimism is actually a little dampened.

The game is probably a bit too easy and routine, once you've cracked the system.

When it's hard, it's hard due to random chance compounding problem with problem (like some of the extreme outcomes in Arkham).

This is still a great game, and it's fun to play. But you will not always get a consistently CHALLENGING experience when you play it.

RATING: 6.7
Light cooperative dungeon delver, with very simple rules and and a really easy to manage AI that provides for some challenging situations. 

The components are top notch: cool miniatures, thick cardboard, nice insert...

It includes an adventure book that contains a dozen adventures. They provide very light background and very few sepecific rules, not really different than anything you can improvise. The best news about this book is that it also provides different game modes, competitive play, team competition, solo adventures, etc. 

The characters are different enough to provide different gaming experiences and to allow for some tactics.

Replayability is not huge. You can easily improvise more adventures, but the feeling is pretty much the same for everyone of them (except the different game modes). You can add components from other D&D games (Legacy of Drizzt, Wrath of Ashardlon) to add some variation.

This is a light and much shorter Descent, for those who want a more fluid game.

First impressions [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/173073/item/3595903#item3595903]here[/url].

RATING: 7.5
This game seemed a little simplistic from the description and the rules that I've read, plus I've gotten into slightly heavier dungeon crawls like Gears of War so I wasn't sure that I'd like it all that much. But wow, it was actually a ton of fun despite its simplicity. It's tough but not overly so, and as long as you pay attention to the AI cards, when they'll activate and what they'll do, you can manage them fairly well. 

The encounters seemed like the toughest part as the random screwage can really mess up your plan. We pulled off a victory with literally one turn to spare on our first play. I think as long as you come in expecting something light and you're able to use a bit of imagination as in regular D&D, this can be a very good experience.

Played Adventure 2 and 6 with my 6 year old. 2 was a walk in the park but we failed at 6. Spent 4 healing surges but give up after 2 of us were dead at the same time. Failed 6 most probably because I was using the Wizard to pair up with the Fighter instead of the Cleric as in Adventure 2.

Didn't care much for the story, didn't care much for my character. Everything felt very mechanical. Realized that for a story-driven game as this, having a campaign is so important for feeling engaged. Although Adventure 6 was more interesting as we got to battle a dragon, the build up to it felt very samey. Open a tile, get new monster, kill monster, get treasure, repeat. Oh and the encounters are real bummers. I guess that's the game's way of making it difficult. 

110417
Played first adventure with Rogue. Didn't like the dull tokens, tiles and cards. Minis were nice though. Mechanically very simple. Draw monster, monster attacks, kill monster, open next tile, repeat. Decisions come in only in deciding whether to use daily and utiity powers. Didn't get a chance to level up hero as it's entirely dependent on luck. I hope that the next adventure with more characters will be more fun. Will compare this directly with Descent 2.0 solo before deciding whether to keep this. Might be a good intro to dungeon crawling with my 6 year old.

RATING: N/A
E02 Red
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A pleasant enough co-operative ride game. It's your standard explore, put out new tiles, kill the monster, repeat until you satisfy the scenario condition type game. You start off by getting to choose your attack methods for your character which provides some inter-game variety. A turn is to move then attack or vice versa, with a new tile revealed afterwards if you're on the edge of the known world, complete with the inevitable new monster that gets a free whack at you. Attacks are adding a single die roll to your chosen attack method and seeing if it's >= the target's hit points, so they're pleasingly quick. There's a bit of ongoing strategic discussion between the players on how best to approach the scenario, how to tackle the monsters currently out, how to manage hitpoints, etc. It's pretty straightforward, do this or do that, but it's generally enough to dissipate the downtime ennui between turns. The decisions each turn aren't much - attack then move, move then attack, which attack method. But at least turns don't get bogged down with special powers or overburdened by modifier calculations, allowing the game to move along nicely and complete in the designated hour or so. The monster process is neat, that they only activate (move and attack as per the rules on its card) on the turn of the player that discovered it, giving the players a full round to deal with it - unless multiples of the same type come out and then they'll all activate each time one activates which can cause some stress. This process, and the encounter cards that regularly come out, create a sense of peril to be overcome, engaging the players. There's not a lot of deepthink, it's certainly played for the ride, but at the end of it, you have the satisfaction of a party collectively completing its mission or a determination to get it done right next time. As such, the game's succeeded in its own mission and I‚Äôd happily play again. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Yup! It's a tight co-op explore and combat board game system set in a D&D universe. Fun and plays in less than 90 minutes, not counting setup. This does well what the game system sets out to do. Basic characters, monsters, and traps. Many scenarios. Wonderful (unpainted) miniatures. Easy to teach. Also plays some solitaire scenarios. Lots of luck involved. I find the scenarios tough to beat.

I cannot say if it satisfies anyone's "dungeon crawl" cravings (which is a very subjective issue), but I have a blast playing this board game.

RATING: 5
"Strips out the best parts of D&D (exploration and story). Beautiful minis and nice dungeon tiles. It ends up being a brutal, random slog. At least it's short."

What I like:
- Quick pure co-op dungeon crawl, with great components
- Lots of scenarios in the box
- Combat system is nicely streamlined

What I don't like:
- Feels VERY oppressive, with monster and/or encounter every single turn
- High randomness with which monsters and events show up (plus dice, of course)

I had played Ashardalon once, with (I think) 5 people, and it felt like a brutal slog. Solo Ravenloft felt that way too. The math just doesn't work for me. Every turn, you're likely to get a monster (who hits you before you can fight back, often for 2 or 3 points), and half the time you'll get an encounter (which is likely to hit you). So you have maybe 10 turns before you're dead, but the fastest you can win is 11 turns. 

I think the BGG-voted 2.5 weight is about right.

RATING: 8
I'm not sure how much this really captures the D&D (4th ed?) experience, since, at least in our plays the plan is to keep drawing monsters and avoid Encounters; thus the game becomes more a case of rushing thru a dungeon, reather than cautious advances etc. However it all works and it's fun.
The game is a lot of fun, scales well across a number of players and has been a hit for all. I do have a question as to it's long term appeal, since the monsters etc become a bit familiar (which, given the rushed nature of the game, means they can become quite abbreviated when they're revealed). Having said that, there is a goodly number of adventures presented in the book and of couree, all the piece are top notch

RATING: 8.75
I have mixed feelings about this game.  It seems very heavily anti-hero, but you REALLY feel like something has been achieved when winning due to the fact almost every rule is weighed against you.  The miniatures are incredibly well done, of course it would be nice if they were pre-painted, but can't get it all :p

Not to thrilled about activating multiple monsters of the same type every villian phase, but again when one manages to overcome this obstacle it feels like a real victory.

I enjoy the fact that the games in this line are combinable, creating a huge pool of heroes and monsters to play with.  Add in the monsters from Dungeon Command and the variety is astounding.

The rewards seem incredibly weak compared to the risk, but in all this creates a very tense game that is incredibly fun to play until losing in the 6th turn because of just plain bad luck drawing!

Giving it an 8 for the great D&D feel and component quality and sheer variety and replayability.  Fun game to play alone or with gamers who have some sense of strategy and teamwork.

RATING: 3
This game was disappointing to me. The person I played with seemed to like it though. 

I found the choices to be too limited. The playing board is almost meaningless. I wonder if this game could work better as a card game. The play feels masochistic as you are always losing health with no way to really avoid damage each turn. Your health serves as a timer and this game mechanic ends up feeling demoralizing. Basically you must complete the end game before your health runs out. Another thing that bothered me was that your special power cards for the most part felt underpowered and are once per game use. Combat is also very random and swingy.

I did like that the game played fast. I really liked the miniatures. I also liked the concept of the game. Unfortunately it felt half baked.

I would say this game is worth getting if you want to use the figures and boards in a D&D 4th edition campaign.

RATING: 8.5
I really like this fully cooperative game. The mechanics are very simple, but the variety brought by the tile placement and the randomness of the monster and event deck make this a different game every time. The missions are varied enough, and even if you limit yourself to what comes in the base mission book, it would pay for itself. This is the game I would recommend for someone who likes the D&D theme but does not like paper and pencil roll playing games. I works well from 1 through 5 players, though I think 4 is the best size (2 players with 2 characters each also works really well). The characters support each other well, and you should play to that. Fun, light and with great components. The only people I think would not go for this game are heavy Descent players. Too light for that type of mentality.

RATING: 7.5
I would probably describe this as a potentially great game
with a number of flaws. No game is perfect, but this game
gets so much right and holds such promise that it's very
disapointing to stop short of real excellence. There
are tons of threads on these issues, so I'll just sum up
a few as I see them: 
1. Thematic elements are vastly underused. A short paragraph at the start of an adventure, no closing info.
Encounters are only a couple sentences and typically just
a quick shot to take HP.
2. The Game system is streamlined to a point it feels very mechanical at times. Not enough choice or variation 
during turns.
3. The Leveling system is very weak and limited.

Some of these issues come from player's expectations. If
you know what this game is about, and it still sounds
good, by all means get it. For what it is, it can be a
very enjoyable dungeon romp. Despite nitpicking, it still
has a very high average rating, because ultimately
players can see a great game is in there somewhere.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Rated after about 6 games. This is a really fun hack and slash, fantasy-oriented game that is becoming quite addictive for me. Wickedly difficult at first, once you get a grip on the flow of play it feels more balanced. That's not to say there aren't wide swings of luck -- there is a D20 involved after all -- but good play will help the luck along and allow more wins. Note that all plays have been solo, which is just fine with me for now.

I'll revisit my rating after a few more plays to see whether it holds up or even raises in estimation.

RATING: 6
This game was - surprisingly - a major disappointment: poor design, cold, simplicistic, with mechanics lacking realism to consider it a dungeoncrawl. Completely uninspired, flavorless... An insult to the Ravenloft world, in my opinion.
Furthermore the rules booklet is not clear and a lot of questions arise during play; ok, some of them may be solved with logic, but many need an official FAQ.
Also components are poor.. few colors, no artwork on cards. Bah. Miniatures are clearly well done, but they are recycled D&D Miniatures after all (and unpainted...!)

A real pity because the game could have had some very good potential being a full cooperative (without a 'Master') with interesting and different scenarios, but as it is it was a waste of money.
One of the worst games I played in the last years.

RATING: 7
Played this game for the first times(s) today and fairly enjoyed it. However, the game is a bit too simple for me and the theme (I loathe the "world" of Dungeons & Dragons) is too generic. The game features your usual random mix of supersimplified post-Tolkien wash outs, ranger,cleric, fighter, skeletons, ghoul etc., nothing new but old ideas recycled into something worse. The game itself is rather repetitive and I am not sure whether this will change with later scenarios (played the 1st and 6th). Furthermore, it looks like WOTC supplied the minimum amount of monsters, scenarios and heroes only to make this heavily expendable and cash in. If you compare the amount of bits and bobs in this game with the similar Descent or Arkham Horror, it looks pathetic. All in all, a nice dungeon romp and probably and nice fantasy-gateway game but unless I get in in a trade, I cannot see myself purchasing it at any point in the future.

RATING: 5
Had the opportunity to play before I bought it and felt good about purchasing it. I still feel that way to this day, but not at the price I paid for it.

At $65, I was expecting a much deeper experience from this game. Uninteresting map tiles without purpose (except on specific encounters), treasure cards with varying degrees of usefulness and big blank areas where artwork should be, and fairly predictable gameplay were the big things that shot down my excitement for the game.

The one thing I must give it credit for is that it can be a somewhat satisfying dungeon crawl experience in only an hour of gameplay. For this reason, I do not mind pulling it out for certain groups of friends and can actually have a good amount of fun playing it with them.

[b]The good:[/b] Intermixing of enemy and player turns, concept behind enemy AI, removal of tedious "line of sight", variety of encounter and power card types, and no "door" mechanic (removes choke point issues when grinding monsters)

[b]The bad:[/b] The artwork of all components are uninspiring, character system is totally void of depth, basic health system: three tries and game over, difficulty not based on player count, and no campaign system.

RATING: 7.3
Solo Player Value = Good.

Two Player Value = Good.

Three Player Value = Good.


Castle Ravenloft is a fun cooperative dungeon crawl game that scales fantastically from a solo player to five players.

Castle Ravenloft carries the Dungeons & Dragons license and uses the general flavor, terms, items, monsters, etc., of Dungeons & Dragons. But this is a different version of Dungeons & Dragons then what many might be accustomed too.

The Dungeons & Dragons system used in Castle Ravenloft has more in common with the card driven Pathfinder system then the tried and true 4th Edition D&D that many know well. That Pathfinder system is then further altered to adapt it to work in a board game. Although altered, the base D&D system is still intact. The base D20 system is present and easy to use. Triggered card abilities power the individual character abilities.

While some might be put-off by this modified D&D system, I think it works well for this game. The D&D system in Castle Ravenloft is easy to learn and manipulate making it very accessible to all levels of gamers.

Essentially Castle Ravenloft is a multi-player solitaire game. Each turn the player gets to perform his player action and them must act as the game against the players. This keeps the game clean and smooth and allows the game to scale well. The constant game actions keeps the game lively and tough on the players which I find challenging and fun.

The art work is nice as the figures are well done. The modular board is standard D&D fair.

The scenarios work very well and scale upward in difficulty.  This offers the players an easy continuing campaign adventure through all the scenarios.


Pro - 
- Great use of the Dungeons & Dragons theme and license 
- Easy to learn and play
- Good dungeon crawl feeling

Cons -
- Modified D&D system might put-off some
- If you do not like dungeon crawl you will like this game


Overall Playability = 7.3

RATING: 10
This is a players vs. the system cooperative game of dungeon crawling and combat. Players choose from 5 different characters each granting a unique ability to the group. Players will select a scenario to play through which will determine the starting position of the board and set out the victory conditions. The map is randomly determined by using a set of square tiles, ensuring that each tome the game is played the experience will be different. 

When monsters are revealed a card is drawn and a figure is placed on the board. The monster's card determines how they act when activated, by listing their tactics.  Players who found the monster "own" the monster and only activate them at the end of their turn, or if another player has an identical monster.  The players lose if anyone dies, and with only 2 healing surges available that is a likely outcome. The game is challenging but not insurmountable nor formulaic.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
 A modern classic - the monster AI was a ground-breaking innovation, much imitated. The game is fast, fun, and beautifully made. The minis are exceptional quality and value. Later iterations implemented a lot of good ideas, but certainly of the first three games I found this the most fun to play - possibly because it is tough, certainly I found the scenarios to be most interesting. The encounters are deadly, so the game can be very challenging to beat - but some combinations of characters are much stronger than others (include the wizard in your party if you want a challenge, and don't have the cleric in play - cleric+fighter is a pretty tough team). I play it more either solo or with kids than with other adults, but have really enjoyed all my plays so far.

RATING: 7
This is a great game and is very well-designed.  It plays in an hour or so, which is a tremendous achievement for a dungeon crawler.  There is such a tendency by game designers to want to emphasize the leveling experience and detail so much that games can drag on forever.  That puts them out the reach of people with limited pay time, like me.  CR is a light tactical game but not so light that there are no meaningful choices.  You have to think and work together to do well.  I would contrast this with, say, Heroquest, which has almost no real choices at all.  I like each choice of powers each character has.  It adds variety that makes things interesting.  Making the powers of different characters work together is interesting too.  There is a lot of player interaction and teamwork as people talk about options.   Anyway, this game is a real achievement.

RATING: 6
Well, I finally played it and to say that I was underwhelmed would be an understatement.  It's like Descent merged with intro D&D 4e.  The bits are all pretty cool, but considering all of the minis are sculpts from previous sets, you'd think they could have included the previous pre-painted minis.  It may have added to the cost a little, but it would have made a big visual difference, rather than looking at blue blobs for heroes and red and ivory blobs for monsters.

Talk about over-hyped.  The gameplay is meh, at best.  Every room has an encounter, the Cleric can BARELY heal anyone, the distinction between tile and space is weird, etc.  Everything pretty much does 1, 2, or 3 points of damage so its weird for anyone who has prior D&D experience.  Line of sight is irrelevant and there are just too many quirks about it that make it something that I have pretty much no interest to play again.  I almost gave it a 7 as our group had a decent enough time with it, but since it is a WotC product, it incurs an immediate 1 point penalty :-)

RATING: 6
This is not a bad game. It's a little repetitive, but I would say it's biggest flaw for me (and where I feel it breaks away from it's D&D theme) is a lack of experience carryover. Even though the scenarios are written as a campaign, each game you start over from scratch. Your character can only ever be a level one or a level two, and you start at level one again each time you play. You also don't keep to keep your equipment. Some of it just goes into a draw pile so you have an extremely small chance of drawing it the next game. Not a terrible game, but definitely doesn't live up to the D&D theme.

RATING: 7
So far, played as a 3-character party. Would have done well to have everyone read the rules first, as there is a lot (by quantity, not complexity) to get through, but we managed fine. Some common questions should be better answered in the booklet (e.g. do coffins impede movement?). RATING: 8.5
The game doesn't seem like much at first: fairly procedural, not much variation in the cards, etc.

But damn, I can't get it out of my head. 

Everything comes together quite well. The gameplay is like a D&D miniatures ultralite combined with Betrayal at House on the Hill exploration and movement.

The pluses are that it's simple. It's doesn't really bog you down with FFG-style kazillion cards and 48 page rules. I guess that you swap simplicity for procedure.

But the theme and variation on the 'missions' makes up for this. Once the players gets a hang of the way the game works, it clicks. 

All scenarios seem to be well balanced and playtested. It's tough but not impossible. Plus the game has a really good system of self-governing and scales well. More players = more monsters and encounters. Each player taking charge of their own monsters and traps means that the game doesn't need a DM (everyone gets to play!).

The characters are quite varied and skills are interesting to see in effect. 

I just enjoy it for what it is. A light dungeon-romp that's over in an hour. 

Looking forward to the Wrath of Ashardon.

Funnily enough, the guy at the local store said Eurogamers hated the game because of the procedure and fixed sequence of play. D&Ders were buying the game for the bits to recreate their campaign!

WOTC: you've got a hit!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I feel that Castle Ravenloft LOOKS a lot more complex than it actually is once it's all set up. It's a little annoying how the board expands because sometimes it tends to go off the table, then we have to migrate to the floor. The minis are really detailed, and I feel that you get a lot for the price. Some scenarios become frustrating because it's easy to lose for reasons that cannot be controlled, such as traps and monsters that can randomly spawn out of control. I find the game to be fun for a light dungeons and dragons game but it really depends on the scenario.

I have a tendency for analysis-paralysis, and this game hacks away at that. That is, for a game that lasts an hour tops, I don't get hung-up on whether this move or that move will wreck a 3- or 4-hour epic.

The game also lends itself well to variants and customization. I'm looking forward to trying out campaign and more-tactical variants, and developing character cards for minis from other games.

RATING: 8.5
Pros:
+ Plays in an hour
+ Customizable heroes
+ Cooperative gameplay
+ Little setup time
+ Scales perfectly with increasing number of players
+ Each game plays differently
+ Rules are easy to learn
+ Modularity of components makes it easy to create custom content


Cons:
- Encounters are always bad and cannot be prevented by exploring
- The dungeon tiles add nothing to the gameplay (e.g. like obstacles/ stairs/ secret passages...)
- Gameplay is sometimes predictable  (e.g. always place a new monster when exploring)
- Monsters always get to strike first
- Artwork is a bit bland, though functional

Overall I think this is a great game! Hoping to see Wrath of Ashardalon remove the last minor flaws.

RATING: 9.5
Fun all over the place, with the right group.

the BITS!! - please, someone paint my minis! 

the short play time

the fantastic scenario book - each adventure really feels different, uses different tokens and boss creatures, and some even throw in different mechanics, such as a day/night timer. 

The player-chosen powers that are unique to each character

The level of difficulty of the adventures - well designed and tense till the end (so far)

The dungeon tiles, which make the board different each play



Some stuff im not a fan of:

I don't like how simple combat is - I see why they did it this way, but ill have to home rule a better combat system, or research a good geek-made one.

the main mechanic of the game, wherebye one player attacks a creature, then can explore, then all the creatures s/he controlls attack,then the next player can attack, feels very disjointed to me. The combat is broken up in such a way that I dont really get the feeling that I and the rest of the characters are really fighting together at the same time. I would like it much more if all the characters could attack, one after the other, then all the creatures attack the characters. But, i know this is just a personal preference. 

There are far too many encounters, and they feel too abstract. I use the Barkams system (found in the variants section) to ameliorate this - works much better. Thanks Barkam!

RATING: 3
It boils down to this: If you like D&D chances are high you will enjoy this, too, if you don't care for D&D this will hardly work for you. Very similar, essentially this game is role playing without a game master and extremely reduced story aspect, you give people cards instead of die rolls to set abilities and powers. And then you enter the dungeon but whereas in in regular D&D (and any other role player) you have a range of choices about the events, here monsters pop up constantly and you try to kill them while not dying so that maybe you can get to live long enough to reach the quest target.
A Co-op that is reasonably tough but highly repetitive. Heroes move only a space while monsters move a complete tile, another little aspect I didn't like.
So, I have tried to get into D&D but it just wasn't for me and neither is this game but if having to pick one I would take regular role playing over this, there is just more going on, this one is the boiled down version and therefor bland. If you think the look of these is great, check out the Dungeon Command series, those look even better and those I enjoy playing!

RATING: 2
I was pretty excited prior to owning this game, however, after a couple of plays I am now selling it.

The game is dull, thematically it is good however this is far overshadowed by a poorly written manual with very ambiguous rules and dull gameplay.
The game relentlessly punishes the player(s) with damage dealing events at every turn. There is little or no chance to regain the number of Hit Points that are lost each round so it is a fight of attrition. Monsters move by tile, players have to move by squares so there isn't even a chance to escape. Monsters attack first, most encounters do damage of some form.
The game wants the party of players to remain bunched up and fight one monster at a time. Even when you do nothing for your turn, something nasty is likely to happen.
This game could have been great, but sadly misses the mark by a vampiric mile.

RATING: 7
2.6 kg
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Very weak.  Played several games where it just felt like the die hated us.  I think after one person rolled a 1 or 2 on 6 attacks in a row, we just gave up.  There wasn't going to be much point to trying to finish that game since we were basically screwed with all the monsters on the board.

Goes far beyond bad luck, though.  The game is clearly stacked against the heroes with many attacks primed to hit most characters except for the fighter with a >50% chance, whereas the heroes' powers have maybe a 50% chance or slightly less to hit any monster that isn't a weak kobold.  It's much easier with more adventurers; I would almost go as far as to say that the game is likely unplayable with two characters because you will die too often.

The worst offender, however, is the combat system.  It seems innovative and unique.  The real problem lies in the mechanics of it.  Having played D&D 4.0 and thinking it has a rather elegant tactical combat grid mechanic, going to a system that parses out attacks orthogonally by tiles seems ridiculous.  I cannot fathom why a monster sitting on a tile diagonally across from me would simply move one tile toward me while a monster seven squares away on the edge of an adjacent tile would zoom all the way over and attack me with all its might.  This major disconnect makes the game feel very stupid and inelegant.  It sucks whatever little theme out that the game had and makes it a contest of trying to position the monsters as strategically as possible so that the game doesn't kill you off too quickly.  No, thanks.

[b]House Rules:[/b]

[b]Set-Up:[/b] Do not start with a treasure card.

[b]Player Turns:[/b]
Heroes have 3 actions that may be taken in any combination in any order. However, a hero can only take each specific action or attack power once per turn.
:PST:Move
:PST:Sprint 3 spaces (a shorter move)
:PST:Explore (otherwise following the normal explore rules)
:PST:Use an Attack power (at-will or daily). A hero MAY make multiple attacks each turn, as long as each one uses a different power or item.
:PST:Use an Item or power that says it ‚Äúreplaces attacks‚Äù or ‚Äúreplaces move‚Äù; this does NOT use up the hero's one move or any specific attack ability for the turn.
:PST:Use an ability that says it can be used ‚Äúif the hero did not attack‚Äù or ‚Äúdid not move‚Äù; the hero MAY move or attack in conjunction with these actions; they just need to take an action to use it.

Heroes do NOT explore automatically at the end of their turn; only by using Explore actions.
A hero must use an action to use an ability that is normally ‚Äúif the hero did not move/attack‚Äù this turn.

[b]Exploration:[/b]
When a hero finds a tile with a white arrow, place a monster on it (or follow scenario rules).
When a hero finds a tile with a black arrow, DO NOT draw an event. Roll 1 die:
1-10: Place an extra monster on the tile (for 2 monsters on the tile normally).
11-20: After placing monster(s) normally, the highest XP monster becomes an Elite monster with 2 extra HP (mark it by giving the monster Hit Point tokens).

[b]Events:[/b]
If a hero does not explore on their turn, draw an event as normal.
Only draw one event per turn, even if multiple sources would trigger an event.
Heroes may spend 5 XP to cancel an event as usual.

[b]Treasure:[/b]
A hero only draws a treasure card for killing a monster worth 2 XP or more.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I'll rate this a 9 for now, but I can see this going to a 10.  It's perfect.  Simple rules, quick playtime, great components.  If you want a dungeon crawler hack-n-slash game that plays in an hour, you simply cannot do any better.  

This is the real deal.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Very accessible tile-laying co-operative dungeon crawler. It's extremely well-produced with an absolute tonne of components, all produced to the highest quality. The game's strength is also its weakness though. It plays fast for a game of this type, which is definitely a good thing and prevents the combat (which is the main feature of the game) from becoming repetitive. This in turn means that you don't get much of an opportunity to develop your character (you can only level-up once and there's a dearth of different skills to customise your character with), which, if done well, is a feature that definitely enhances the appeal of RPG-themed games. Also, considering that there's so much included in the game, there doesn't feel as though there's an awful lot of variety to the characters (only five to choose from), monsters and playable adventures thus limiting the game's replayability somewhat. I do expect much of that to change with future expansions though, which this game seems to have a great deal of potential for.

RATING: 3
Saw the theme, looked amazing.  Dungeon crawler, sounded good.  Coop, no overlord with options for single player gaming, sounded really good.

Anyway bought the game and was really really disappointed. 

It just feels too basic, too repetitive.  It's like the moment you start playing you're just dying and it's about trying to die as slow as possible, because then you just might finish the quest.
Move, grab a tile, put monster on tile, draw encounter - lose health, monster attacks - possibly lose more health.  Then you get to attack.  It'd be miles better if the monster didn't always get the drop on you, like if you had some mechanic there to give you a chance to surprise attack but nope.  Move, draw tile, monster goes on tile, draw encounter, monster attacks, you attack, next person does the same.

I think a big part of the disappointment was the theme looks amazing and the miniatures are really cool.  The tiles are too similar and the encounters could just omit all the flavour text and just put a number of how much health i'm going to lose.  

I think this one needs a revised edition really bad, redo the artwork on the tiles to make the rooms look different - there's really not much going on in most of those tiles.
  
Rework the mechanics to make the gameplay feel a little deeper, heck, even if they just made it so that I could get the drop on a monster sometimes would be better and to offset it they could make some of the monsters hit a bit harder or have more health.

Even though i've come down on it pretty hard (probably because i was overly hyped) I'm still tempted to hold on to this one just on theme alone, oh and the really really cool miniatures, just in case (this would be my dream come true) they release an expansion that turns it into castle ravenloft advanced! same cool theme, same cool miniatures, just new rules to make it feel like there's more going on there, oh and some new floor tiles to make it look less generic.  Oh please let that come true ...

RATING: 6.5
Not the really thematic game I was hoping for.

A good cooperative game that has the plus of the D&D theme. The 4th edition rules work really well in the board game. The production value is high - the minis are beautiful and the components give your money worth.

There is a good deal of luck in the game (dice rolls, blind draft of enemies, treasures, and so forth), that isn't far off what you will encounter in many RPG sessions.

This, nonetheless, isn't an RPG. No roleplay is required.

Being a mission-based game usually meant that the game isn't all that high in the replay value department, but since the game has a lot of random aspects, I guess this game can have more replay value than usual.

RATING: 9
We played this game about 5 times in one weekend.  Incredibly fun for the genre and streamlined.

Each player character has its own class with special abilities.  The minis are WAY cooler than chits, but it would have been ideal to have them painted.

The Adventures are creative and NOT repetitive.  It leaves you with the feeling of wanting more of it...expansions would be warmly received.

If you play Dungeons and Dragons and are a boardgamer, you will probably eat this game up, and ask for more please.  

The greatest innovation this rule set brings to the genre is the seamless elimination of the Dungeon Master, which allows EVERYONE to play as player-characters/adventurers. This works incredibly well, but of course it also eliminates the interactive story telling that comes along with role playing.

I really can't wait to see where WOTC takes this build.  I haven't been this excited about a boardgame (other than Summoners Wars) in a long time.

There were some ambiguities in the rulebook, so I docked it one point.  Otherwise this game is a clear 10 for me.

RATING: 5
There's really nothing wrong with this game.  It works pretty well, and from what I hear, it captures the essence of D&D 4th edition pretty well, so there are two things going for it.  I think I'm just over the whole cooperative game craze, at least if you're not playing them with the right people.  There's something missing from the game if a player draws an encounter card and just says, "We all lose one hit point."  Why?  What happened?  What did we encounter that made us lose a hit point?  And if I'm asking the question, the proper response isn't "It doesn't matter"; this game is about theme, goddammit, so give me my theme!

I'll play this again, and try it with some different people if I can, but for now, it's just a fancy-looking cooperative game that falls into a long line of fancy-looking cooperative games.

1 play: 5 players

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The game's primary draw is that it is one of the few deep-dungeon adventures that finishes in an hour, and it does so in an all-for-one musketeer style that is pleasantly entertaining at worst and silly fun at best.

Make no mistake, though. There is nothing even remotely sophisticated about Ravenloft.  It revels in its low-strategy hijinks, and it does so with gusto.  But it does have moments of unexpected laughter, and it can be knocked out in the same amount of time that it takes to don your embarrassing LARP outfit - which is something that no other game in the genre can even come close to claiming.

There will be unfortunate instances where the game is as short as 15 minutes long, especially if your party stumbles into a slate of bone-rending traps from the dreaded Encounter deck. But since it's all in good cooperative fun, you and your family/friends can commiserate about your bad luck with dice and collectively look forward to launching another underground expedition in the near future.  Like in a few minutes, if you have the stamina to play again.

Played solely by adults, Ravenloft can tickle your hack-and-slash sensibilities, but whether you'd want to play it more than a few times with an older crowd is up for debate. Based on a handful of plays with my kids, it seems to have more staying power as a family game.  So while I don't see adults calling for the game regularly, if I'm in the mood for goofy card titles, off-kilter flavor text, and incessant monster attacks, then I'll be the first to grab my copy off the shelf and set it up.

And at the on-line price, it's definitely a bargain - especially if you've got a hankering for a skin-deep experience that contains as much haphazard fantasy as an old school Bag of Holding. Ah, nostalgia.

[Rated mostly as a family game.]

RATING: 4
I have to say that I really didn't enjoy this game as much as I thought I would. My 21 year old daughter and I gave up just before the second adventure was completed. We found the rules, cards and counters too fiddly and the game play too complicated to run smoothly. We were constantly forgetting the order of play and all of the conditional rules on the cards. We'd realize too late that we had forgotten to play a certain card or move a counter and we ran into questions that the rules could not clarify. We eventually got weary of being beaten down by monsters and encounter cards as we made our way through the castle which lacked the atmosphere of our beloved HeroQuest. In retrospect , I don't think the cooperative nature of the game really suits the dungeon crawl experience as you have to constantly alternate between your character and the evil things that attack it.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.18
That is a very thematic game assured to score high with D&D fans. The components are great, with lots of miniatures giving a great feel to a game and lots of scenarios to be played and replayed. Even though the price tag is high, it is quite worth the fun it provides.
The rules are very easy to grasp and do not take forever to be memorized. However the rule book needs some proofreading as some points are poorly organised. It ends with a full faq page, which shows that something is due about it at some time.
The various characters are fun even though one dimensional, but that is a board game, not a rpg. That contributes to make of the game an excellent appetizer for a full blow rpg experience.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A light dungeon crawl game that doesn't need a DM, which in my opinion is a great blessing. I've been playing RPGs for over 15 years and I can count the number of times I haven't been DM on two hands.

I have few plays for now but there's enough variety in there that I plan for the game to remain fresh for a good while. Further, it's likely to be well supported in the next few years through DDI and added expansions.

It's been well liked by all players I've tried it with.

RATING: 6.8
Fun but feels thin; not enough depth in the game system. Severe lack of unique abilities. Punished for everything you do. Nothing good literally ever happens to your characters....it almost feels defeating before you even start. Still a great way to scratch your D&D itch. I don't think this is something I will get on the table often...it's missing that "fun/excitement factor." I would play this again though as I enjoy the theme and it plays relatively quickly.

Easy enough to teach casual gamers. Components are top notch, and it looks impressive on the table. It definitely is missing something though; explorations and rewards?

HUGE potential for house rules and variants though. Not my sort of thing normally but I may give it a shot and come up with something interesting.

RATING: 6.5
Castle Ravenloft is a very fun and addictive game. It will keep you on the edge of your seat. I'm a big fan of the adventure system that they've created with this and I can't wait to see how it will evolve over time with newer titles. The ever changing dungeons with the tiles is a really cool idea that allows the same scenerio to be different in a way each time it's played through.The 60 minutes play time is a big bonus and the ability to combined this with WoA is another huge plus.

RATING: 2
Just awful, every turn felt like the game was punishing you to the extent you did not feel like you were exploring a dungeon but instead were trapped in a punishment simulator.  DungeonQuest, a game where you can die on your very first turn through no fault of your own, was much better at simulating a dungeon exploration game, and that is far from a highly rated game.

Ravenloft has great components and, despite appearances when you take it out of the box, does take about an hour to play.  Multiple different scenarios add variety to the game.

If you played this game and felt slightly better about it than I did, you might want to try Wrath of Ashardalon as it somewhat improves on what I feel are the deep flaws of this game.
